mw-body-content">\n</div>\n<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Ming treasure voyages</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/w/index.php?title=Treasure_voyages&amp;redirect=no" title="Treasure voyages">Treasure voyages</a>)</span></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Zheng He Xia Xiyang" redirects here. For the TV series about the voyages, see <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He_Xia_Xiyang_(TV_series)" title="Zheng He Xia Xiyang (TV series)">Zheng He Xia Xiyang (TV series)</a>.</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<table class="infobox" style="width:22em"><tbody><tr style="display:none;"><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size: 125%; background-color:#b0c4de;"><b>Ming treasure voyages</b></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ZhengHeShips.gif"><img alt="ZhengHeShips.gif" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="426" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/ZhengHeShips.gif/260px-ZhengHeShips.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/ZhengHeShips.gif/390px-ZhengHeShips.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7f/ZhengHeShips.gif 2x" width="260"/></a><div>Several of Zheng He\'s ships as depicted on a woodblock print, early 17th century</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_characters" title="Traditional Chinese characters">Traditional&#160;Chinese</a></th><td><span lang="zh-Hant" style="font-size:1rem;" title="Chinese language text">&#37165;&#21644;&#19979;&#35199;&#27915;</span></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">Simplified Chinese</a></th><td><span lang="zh-Hans" style="font-size:1rem;" title="Chinese language text">&#37073;&#21644;&#19979;&#35199;&#27915;</span></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;">Literal meaning</th><td>[Voyages of] Zheng He down the Western Ocean</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><table class="collapsible collapsed" style="padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;font-size: 100%; text-align: left; background-color: #f9ffbc;">Transcriptions</th></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color: #dcffc9;"><a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Mandarin</a></th></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hanyu_Pinyin" title="Hanyu Pinyin">Hanyu Pinyin</a></th><td><span lang="zh-Latn" style="font-style:normal" title="Chinese-language transliteration">Zh&#232;ng H&#233; Xi&#224; X&#299;y&#225;ng</span></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;background-color: #dcffc9;"><a href="/wiki/Southern_Min" title="Southern Min">Southern Min</a></th></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="font-weight:normal;"><a href="/wiki/Hokkien" title="Hokkien">Hokkien</a> <a href="/wiki/Pe%CC%8Dh-%C5%8De-j%C4%AB" title="Pe&#781;h-&#333;e-j&#299;">POJ</a></th><td><span lang="nan-Latn" style="font-style:normal" title="Taiwanese Hokkien-language transliteration">T&#275;&#8319; H&#244; H&#257; Se-i&#251;&#8319;</span></td></tr></tbody></table></td></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>The <b>Ming treasure voyages</b> were the seven maritime expeditions by <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming China</a>\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_treasure_fleet" title="Chinese treasure fleet">treasure fleet</a> between 1405 and 1433. The <a href="/wiki/Yongle_Emperor" title="Yongle Emperor">Yongle Emperor</a> initiated the construction of the treasure fleet in 1403. The grand project resulted in seven far-reaching ocean voyages to the coastal territories and islands in and around the <a href="/wiki/South_China_Sea" title="South China Sea">South China Sea</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a>, and beyond. Admiral <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">Zheng He</a> was commissioned to command the treasure fleet for the expeditions. Six of the voyages occurred during the Yongle reign (r. 1402&#8211;24), while the seventh voyage occurred under the <a href="/wiki/Xuande_Emperor" title="Xuande Emperor">Xuande</a> reign (r. 1425&#8211;1435). The first three voyages reached up to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calicut" title="Calicut">Calicut</a> on India\'s <a href="/wiki/Malabar_Coast" title="Malabar Coast">Malabar Coast</a>, while the fourth voyage went as far as <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Hormuz" title="Strait of Hormuz">Hormuz</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a>. Afterwards, the fleet made voyages farther away to the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> and <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a>.\n</p><p>The Chinese expeditionary fleet was heavily militarized and carried great amounts of treasures, which served to project Chinese power and wealth to the known world. They brought back many foreign ambassadors whose kings and rulers were willing to declare themselves tributaries of China. During the course of the voyages, they <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Palembang_(1407)" title="Battle of Palembang (1407)">destroyed Chen Zuyi\'s pirate fleet</a> at Palembang, <a href="/wiki/Ming%E2%80%93Kotte_War" title="Ming&#8211;Kotte War">conquered the Sinhalese Kotte kingdom</a> of King Alekeshvara, and defeated the forces of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semudera" title="Semudera">Semudera</a> pretender Sekandar in northern <a href="/wiki/Sumatra" title="Sumatra">Sumatra</a>. The Chinese maritime exploits brought many foreign countries into the nation\'s tributary system and sphere of influence through both military and political supremacy, thus incorporating the states into the greater Chinese world order under Ming <a href="/wiki/Suzerainty" title="Suzerainty">suzerainty</a>. Moreover, the Chinese structured and established control over an <a href="/wiki/Maritime_Silk_Road" title="Maritime Silk Road">expansive maritime network</a> in which the region became <a href="/wiki/Regional_integration" title="Regional integration">integrated</a> and its countries became interconnected on an economic and political level. \n</p><p>The treasure voyages were commanded and overseen by the eunuch establishment whose political influence was heavily dependent on imperial favor. However, within Ming China\'s imperial state system, the civil officials were the primary political opponents of the eunuchs and the opposing faction against the expeditions. Around the end of the maritime voyages, the civil government gained the upper hand within the state bureaucracy, while the eunuchs gradually fell out of favor after the death of the Yongle Emperor.\n</p><p>Over the course of the maritime voyages of the early 15th century, Ming China became the <a href="/wiki/Naval_history_of_China" title="Naval history of China">pre-eminent naval power</a> by projecting its sea-power further to the south and west. There is still much debate about issues such as the actual purpose of the voyages, the size of the ships, the magnitude of the fleet, the routes taken, the nautical charts employed, the countries visited, and the cargo carried.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#Bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Background">Background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ming_treasure_voyages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Yongle_Emperor" title="Yongle Emperor">Yongle Emperor</a> inherited a powerful navy from his predecessor, the <a href="/wiki/Hongwu_Emperor" title="Hongwu Emperor">Hongwu Emperor</a>, who himself had great appreciation for naval power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r97a-65-6_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-r97a-65-6-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-l58-49-0_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-l58-49-0-3">[3]</a></sup> The Yongle Emperor further strengthened and expanded the Ming navy as an instrument for an expansive overseas policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r97a-65-6_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-r97a-65-6-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-l58-49-0_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-l58-49-0-3">[3]</a></sup> The <i>Taizong Shilu</i> contains 24 short entries for the imperial orders for shipbuilding, with figures pointing to at least 2868 ships, from 1403 to 1419.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-116-7_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-d7-116-7-4">[4]</a></sup> Over the course of 1403, <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jiangxi" title="Jiangxi">Jiangxi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zhejiang" title="Zhejiang">Zhejiang</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Huguang" title="Huguang">Huguang</a>\'s provincial governments as well as <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Suzhou" title="Suzhou">Suzhou</a>, and other cities\' military garrisons were ordered to begin construction of ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-4950_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-4950-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Under the reign of the <a href="/wiki/Yongle_Emperor" title="Yongle Emperor">Yongle Emperor</a>, Ming China underwent a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty_military_conquests" title="Ming dynasty military conquests">militaristic expansionism</a> with ventures such as the treasure voyages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mil70-1_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-mil70-1-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> In 1403, the Yongle Emperor issued an imperial order to start the immense construction project of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_treasure_fleet" title="Chinese treasure fleet">treasure fleet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev96-734_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-lev96-734-8">[8]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">Zheng He</a> was ordered to initiate the construction of the fleet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-60-1_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-60-1-9">[9]</a></sup> The treasure fleet was known by its original designation "Xiafan Guanjun" (&#19979;&#30058;&#23448;&#36557;; lit. "foreign expeditionary armada") in Chinese sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> It would come to comprise many trading ships, warships, and support vessels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev96-734_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-lev96-734-8">[8]</a></sup> Many of the fleet\'s ships were built at the Longjiang shipyard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-501_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-501-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> All of the treasure ships were also built there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-501_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-501-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-br98-616_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-br98-616-13">[13]</a></sup> The shipyard was located on the <a href="/wiki/Qinhuai_River" title="Qinhuai River">Qinhuai River</a> near <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a>, where it flows into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yangtze_River" title="Yangtze River">Yangtze River</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-501_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-501-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Many trees were cut along the <a href="/wiki/Min_River_(Fujian)" title="Min River (Fujian)">Min River</a> and upper reaches of the Yangtze to supply the necessary resources for the construction of the fleet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-4950_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-4950-5">[5]</a></sup> The Yongle Emperor placed great trust in <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">Zheng He</a> and appointed him to command the treasure fleet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-4950_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-4950-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev96-87_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-lev96-87-15">[15]</a></sup> The emperor even gave Zheng He blank scrolls with the imperial seal, so the admiral could issue imperial orders at sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev96-87_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-lev96-87-15">[15]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Course">Course</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ming_treasure_voyages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Course">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Ming_treasure_voyages" title="Timeline of the Ming treasure voyages">Timeline of the Ming treasure voyages</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_voyage">First voyage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ming_treasure_voyages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: First voyage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Admiral_Zhenghe.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="2304" decoding="async" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Admiral_Zhenghe.jpg/220px-Admiral_Zhenghe.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Admiral_Zhenghe.jpg/330px-Admiral_Zhenghe.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Admiral_Zhenghe.jpg/440px-Admiral_Zhenghe.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Admiral_Zhenghe.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A modern wax statue of Admiral Zheng He (<a href="/wiki/Quanzhou_Maritime_Museum" title="Quanzhou Maritime Museum">Quanzhou Maritime Museum</a>)</div></div></div>\n<p>In the third lunar month (30 March to 28 April) of 1405, a preliminary order was issued for Zheng He and others to take command of 27,000 troops to the Western Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> An imperial edict, dated to 11 July 1405, was issued containing the order for the expedition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-10_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-10-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-duy8b-356_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-duy8b-356-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chu8-2354_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-chu8-2354-19">[19]</a></sup> It was addressed to <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">Zheng He</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wang_Jinghong" title="Wang Jinghong">Wang Jinghong</a>, and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-10_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-10-17">[17]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Yongle Emperor held a banquet for the crew on the evening before the fleet\'s <a href="/wiki/Maiden_voyage" title="Maiden voyage">maiden voyage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev96-89_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-lev96-89-20">[20]</a></sup> Gifts were presented to the officers and the common crew according to their rank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev96-89_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-lev96-89-20">[20]</a></sup> Sacrifices and prayers were offered to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tianfei" title="Tianfei">Tianfei</a>, the patron goddess of sailors, hoping to ensure a successful journey and a safe passage during the voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev96-89_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-lev96-89-20">[20]</a></sup> In the autumn of 1405, the treasure fleet had assembled at <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a> and was ready to depart from the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> According to the <i>Taizong shilu</i> 11 July 1405 entry about the dispatch of the fleet, Admiral Zheng He and "others" departed for the first expedition "bearing imperial letters to the countries of the Western Ocean and with gifts to their kings of gold <a href="/wiki/Brocade" title="Brocade">brocade</a>, patterned <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silks</a>, and colored silk gauze, according to their status."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-51_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-51-22">[22]</a></sup> The treasure fleet made a stop at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liujiagang" title="Liujiagang">Liujiagang</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-512_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-512-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-233_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-233-24">[24]</a></sup> There, the fleet was organized in squadrons, while the fleet\'s crew honored the goddess of sailors <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tianfei" title="Tianfei">Tianfei</a> with prayers and sacrifices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-512_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-512-23">[23]</a></sup> Afterwards, the fleet sailed down the Chinese coast,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-233_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-233-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-52_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-52-25">[25]</a></sup> towards the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Min_River_(Fujian)" title="Min River (Fujian)">Min River</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-52_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-52-25">[25]</a></sup> where they awaited the northeast monsoon at Taiping anchorage in the Changle district.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-52_25-2"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-52-25">[25]</a></sup> More prayers and sacrifices were conducted for Tianfei by the crew during the wait for the northeast monsoon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-52_25-3"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-52-25">[25]</a></sup> Afterwards, the fleet departed via the Wuhumen (lit. "five tiger passage") in Fujian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-52_25-4"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-52-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-358_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-du38-358-26">[26]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The treasure fleet sailed to <a href="/wiki/Champa" title="Champa">Champa</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-233_24-2"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-233-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-52_25-5"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-52-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-358-0_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-du38-358-0-27">[27]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-233_24-3"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-233-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-358-0_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-du38-358-0-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-53_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-53-28">[28]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Malacca" title="Malacca">Malacca</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-233_24-4"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-233-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-358-0_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-du38-358-0-27">[27]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aru_Islands" title="Aru Islands">Aru</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-358-0_27-3"><a href="#cite_note-du38-358-0-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-53_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-53-28">[28]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semudera" title="Semudera">Semudera</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-233_24-5"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-233-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-358-0_27-4"><a href="#cite_note-du38-358-0-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-53_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-53-28">[28]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lambri" title="Lambri">Lambri</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-358-0_27-5"><a href="#cite_note-du38-358-0-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-53_28-3"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-53-28">[28]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ceylon" title="Ceylon">Ceylon</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-233_24-6"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-233-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-358-0_27-6"><a href="#cite_note-du38-358-0-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-53_28-4"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-53-28">[28]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quilon" title="Quilon">Quilon</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-233_24-7"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-233-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-358-0_27-7"><a href="#cite_note-du38-358-0-27">[27]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calicut" title="Calicut">Calicut</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-233_24-8"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-233-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-358-0_27-8"><a href="#cite_note-du38-358-0-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev96-88_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-lev96-88-29">[29]</a></sup> From Lambri, the treasure fleet sailed straight through the Indian Ocean rather than follow the Bay of Bengal coastline to Ceylon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-53_28-5"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-53-28">[28]</a></sup> Three days after the departure from Lambri, a ship split off and went to the Andaman and Nicobar Islands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-53_28-6"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-53-28">[28]</a></sup> The treasure fleet saw the mountains of Ceylon after another six days and arrived at Ceylon\'s western coast two days thereafter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-53_28-7"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-53-28">[28]</a></sup> They were met with a hostile attitude from <a href="/wiki/Alagakkonara" title="Alagakkonara">Alagakkonara</a>, so they left the place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Dreyer (2007) states that it is possible that Admiral Zheng He made port at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quilon" title="Quilon">Quilon</a>&#8212;but there is no account confirming this&#8212;because the King of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quilon" title="Quilon">Quilon</a> traveled with the fleet to China in 1407.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-54_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-54-31">[31]</a></sup> Mills (1970) states that the fleet may have made a four-month stay at Calicut, probably from December to April 1407.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-123_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-d7-123-32">[32]</a></sup> Around Cape Comorin at the southern tip of the Indian Peninsula, the treasure fleet changed direction and began its return journey to China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-54_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-54-31">[31]</a></sup> During the return, the fleet made port at Malacca again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-55_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-55-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1407, while returning homewards, Admiral Zheng He and his associates <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Palembang_(1407)" title="Battle of Palembang (1407)">engaged Chen Zuyi and his pirate fleet in battle</a> at Palembang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-358-0_27-9"><a href="#cite_note-du38-358-0-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-233_24-9"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-233-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-55_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-55-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ray87-pi_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-ray87-pi-34">[34]</a></sup> Chen Zuyi was a pirate leader who had seized Palembang on Sumatra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-233_24-10"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-233-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-55_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-55-33">[33]</a></sup> He dominated the maritime route of the Malaccan Strait.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-233_24-11"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-233-24">[24]</a></sup> The battle resulted in the defeat of Chen\'s pirate fleet by the Chinese treasure fleet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-233_24-12"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-233-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ray87-pi_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-ray87-pi-34">[34]</a></sup> Chen Zuyi and his lieutenants were executed on 2 October 1407.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-59_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-59-35">[35]</a></sup> The Ming court appointed <a href="/wiki/Shi_Jinqing" title="Shi Jinqing">Shi Jinqing</a> as the Pacification Superintendent of Palembang, thereby establishing an ally at Palembang and securing access to this important port.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-se16-63_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-se16-63-36">[36]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The fleet arrived back in Nanjing on 2 October 1407.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-duy8b-356_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-duy8b-356-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-10-1_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-10-1-38">[38]</a></sup> After accompanying the treasure fleet during the return journey, the foreign envoys (from <a href="/wiki/Kozhikode" title="Kozhikode">Calicut</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quilon" title="Quilon">Quilon</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semudera" title="Semudera">Semudera</a>, Aru, <a href="/wiki/Malacca" title="Malacca">Malacca</a>, and other unspecified nations) visited the Ming court to pay homage and present tribute in their local products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-10_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-10-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-59_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-59-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-360_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-du38-360-39">[39]</a></sup> The Yongle Emperor ordered the Ministry of Rites, whose duties included the protocol concerning foreign ambassadors, to prepare gifts for the foreign kings who had sent envoys to the court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-59_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-59-35">[35]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_voyage">Second voyage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ming_treasure_voyages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Second voyage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anonymous-Ming_Chengzu.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2063" data-file-width="1387" decoding="async" height="327" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Anonymous-Ming_Chengzu.jpg/220px-Anonymous-Ming_Chengzu.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Anonymous-Ming_Chengzu.jpg/330px-Anonymous-Ming_Chengzu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Anonymous-Ming_Chengzu.jpg/440px-Anonymous-Ming_Chengzu.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anonymous-Ming_Chengzu.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Painting of the <a href="/wiki/Yongle_Emperor" title="Yongle Emperor">Yongle Emperor</a> (<a href="/wiki/National_Palace_Museum" title="National Palace Museum">National Palace Museum</a>)</div></div></div>\n<p>The imperial order for the second voyage was issued in October 1407.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[a]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-233_24-13"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-233-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-11_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-11-41">[40]</a></sup> The edict was addressed to Zheng He, Wang Jinghong, and Hou Xian (&#20399;&#39023;).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-11_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-11-41">[40]</a></sup> Lang Ying\'s <i>Qixiuleigao</i> (&#19971;&#20462;&#39006;&#31295;) recorded that Zheng He, Wang Jinghong, and Hou Xian were dispatched in 1407.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-duy38-362_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-duy38-362-42">[41]</a></sup> The <i>Taizong Shilu</i> recorded that Zheng He and others went as envoys to the countries of Calicut, Malacca, Semudera, Aru, <i>Jiayile</i>, Java, Siam, Champa, Cochin, <i>Abobadan</i>, Quilon, Lambri, and <i>Ganbali</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-63_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-63-43">[42]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 30 October 1407, a grand director was dispatched with a squadron to Champa before Zheng He followed with the main body of the fleet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup> The fleet departed in the fifth year, late 1407 or possibly early 1408, of the Yongle reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-duy8b-356_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-duy8b-356-18">[18]</a></sup> The fleet traveled from Nanjing to Liujiagang to Changle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-64_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-64-45">[44]</a></sup> They then sailed to Champa; Siam; Java; Malacca; Semudera, Aru, and Lambri on Sumatra; <i>Jiayile</i>, <i>Abobadan</i>, <i>Ganbali</i>, Quilon, Cochin, and Calicut in India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-64_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-64-45">[44]</a></sup> Dreyer (2007) states that it is possible that Siam and Java were visited by the main fleet or by detached squadrons before regrouping at Malacca.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-64_45-2"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-64-45">[44]</a></sup> During this voyage, Zheng He and his fleet did not land on Ceylon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-64_45-3"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-64-45">[44]</a></sup> The fleet was tasked to carry out the formal investure of Mana Vikraan (&#39340;&#37027;&#24517;&#21152;&#21068;&#28415;) as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Kozhikode_Samoodiris" title="List of Kozhikode Samoodiris">the King of Calicut</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev96-88_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-lev96-88-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-11_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-11-41">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-3589_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-du38-3589-46">[45]</a></sup> A tablet was placed in Calicut to commemorate the relationship between China and India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev96-88_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-lev96-88-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-11_41-3"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-11-41">[40]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In this voyage, the Chinese would forcibly settle the enmity between Ming China and Java.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-63_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-63-43">[42]</a></sup> Java was ruled by successive <a href="/wiki/Majapahit" title="Majapahit">Majapahit</a> kings who had acted defiantly towards Ming China since the <a href="/wiki/Hongwu_Emperor" title="Hongwu Emperor">Hongwu reign</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-53_28-8"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-53-28">[28]</a></sup> Dreyer (2007) states that, during a civil war on Java between 1401 and 1406, the King of West Java killed 170 members of a Chinese embassy who had come ashore in his rival\'s territory at East Java.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[b]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-63_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-63-43">[42]</a></sup> The entry dated to 23 October 1407 in the <i>Ming Shilu</i> states that the Western King of Java had sent an envoy to the Ming court to admit guilt for mistakenly killing 170 Ming troops who had gone ashore to trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-se16-613-4_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-se16-613-4-48">[46]</a></sup> It further states that the Ming court responded by demanding 60,000 <i><a href="/wiki/Tael" title="Tael">liang</a></i> of gold for compensation and atonement, warning that they would dispatch an army to punish the Javanese ruler for his crime if he failed to comply, and stating that the <a href="/wiki/Ming%E2%80%93H%E1%BB%93_War" title="Ming&#8211;H&#7891; War">situation in Annam</a> (referring to Ming China\'s successful invasion of Vietnam) can serve as an example.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-se16-613-4_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-se16-613-4-48">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-63_43-3"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-63-43">[42]</a></sup> The Chinese accepted the payment and apology, and restored diplomatic relations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-271-2_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-271-2-49">[47]</a></sup> They would use the voyages to keep surveillance over Java.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-271-2_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-271-2-49">[47]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the journey, the fleet visited the <a href="/wiki/Similan_Islands" title="Similan Islands">Pulau Sembilan</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Malacca" title="Strait of Malacca">Strait of Malacca</a> in the seventh year of the Yongle reign (1409), according to <a href="/wiki/Fei_Xin" title="Fei Xin">Fei Xin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-duy38-362_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-duy38-362-42">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-64_45-4"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-64-45">[44]</a></sup> He stated that the troops were sent there to cut wood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-duy38-362_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-duy38-362-42">[41]</a></sup> Dreyer (2007) concluded that the stop was during the return journey of the second voyage as the treasure fleet did not leave the Chinese coast for the third voyage until early 1410.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-64_45-5"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-64-45">[44]</a></sup> Fei Xin\'s written words were: "In the seventh year of Yongle, Zheng He and his associates sent government troops onto the island to cut incense. They obtained six logs, each eight or nine <i>chi</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zhangchi_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-zhangchi-50">[c]</a></sup> in diameter and six or seven <i>zhang</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-zhangchi_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-zhangchi-50">[c]</a></sup> in length, whose aroma was pure and far-ranging. The pattern [of the wood] was black, with fine lines. The people of the island opened their eyes wide and stuck out their tongues in astonishment, and were told that \'We are the soldiers of the Heavenly Court, and our awe-inspiring power is like that of the gods.\'"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[48]</a></sup> The treasure fleet arrived back in Nanjing in the summer of 1409.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-233_24-14"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-233-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-64_45-6"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-64-45">[44]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The possible confusion of whether Zheng He undertook the second voyage stemmed from the fact that a Chinese envoy was dispatched before Zheng He had departed with the main body of the fleet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-59_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-59-35">[35]</a></sup> The imperial edict for the third voyage was issued during the period of the second voyage whilst the treasure fleet was still in the Indian Ocean, so either Zheng He was absent when the court issued the imperial order or he had not accompanied the fleet during the second voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-66_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-66-52">[49]</a></sup> On 21 January 1409, a grand ceremony was held in the honor of the goddess Tianfei, where she received a new title.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dre7-71_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-dre7-71-53">[50]</a></sup> Duyvendak thinks that Zheng He could not have been on the second voyage, because this ceremony was so important that it required Zheng He\'s attendance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dre7-71_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-dre7-71-53">[50]</a></sup> Mills (1970), citing Duyvendak, also states that Zheng He did not accompany the fleet for this voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-11_41-4"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-11-41">[40]</a></sup> However, Fei Xin explicitly mentions Zheng He when describing the 1409 stop at Pulau Sembilan, which strongly suggests that Zheng He had been on the second voyage according to Dreyer (2007).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[51]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Third_voyage">Third voyage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ming_treasure_voyages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Third voyage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="border-width: 1px; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303512">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{margin:0.5em auto 0.8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft p,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright p{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:larger;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" &#8220; ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" &#8221; ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:360px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{min-width:100%;margin:0 0 0.8em!important;float:none!important}}</style>\n<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">Straight-away, their dens and hideouts we ravaged, <br/> And made captive that entire country, <br/> Bringing back to our august capital, <br/> Their women, children, families and retainers, leaving not one, <br/> Cleaning out in a single sweep those noxious pests, as if winnowing chaff from grain... <br/> These insignificant worms, deserving to die ten thousand times over, trembling in fear... <br/> Did not even merit the punishment of Heaven. <br/> Thus the august emperor spared their lives, <br/> And they humbly kowtowed, making crude sounds and <br/> Praising the sage-like virtue of the imperial Ming ruler.</div>\n<p><cite class="right-aligned" style="">&#8212;  <i>Yang Rong (1515) about the conflict in Ceylon</i> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[52]</a></sup></cite>\n</p>\n</div>\n<p>The imperial order for the third voyage was issued on the first month of the seventh year of the Yongle reign (16 January to 14 February 1409).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-65_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-65-56">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-11-2_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-11-2-58">[55]</a></sup> It was addressed to Zheng He, Wang Jinghong, and Hou Xian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-65_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-65-56">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-11-2_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-11-2-58">[55]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Admiral Zheng He embarked for this voyage in 1409.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-duy38-361-2_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-duy38-361-2-59">[56]</a></sup> The fleet departed from Liujiagang in the ninth month (9 October to 6 November 1409) and arrived at Changle in the following month (7 November to 6 December).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-66_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-66-52">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-11-2_58-2"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-11-2-58">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-duy38-361-2_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-duy38-361-2-59">[56]</a></sup> They left Changle in the twelfth month (5 January to 3 February 1410) for the seas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-66_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-66-52">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-duy38-361-2_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-duy38-361-2-59">[56]</a></sup> They proceeded through the Wuhumen (at the entrance of the Min River in Fujian).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-11-2_58-3"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-11-2-58">[55]</a></sup> The fleet made stops at Champa, Java, Malacca, Semudera, Ceylon, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quilon" title="Quilon">Quilon</a>, Cochin, and Calicut.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-233_24-15"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-233-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-66_52-3"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-66-52">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-duv38-373_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-duv38-373-60">[57]</a></sup> They traveled to Champa within 10 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-66_52-4"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-66-52">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-11-2_58-4"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-11-2-58">[55]</a></sup> Wang Jinghong and Hou Xian made short stops at Siam, Malacca, Semudera, and Ceylon during detours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-233_24-16"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-233-24">[24]</a></sup> Arriving in Ceylon in 1410, the treasure fleet landed at Galle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-66_52-5"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-66-52">[49]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the homeward journey in 1411, the treasure fleet would engage into a <a href="/wiki/Ming%E2%80%93Kotte_War" title="Ming&#8211;Kotte War">military confrontation with King Alakeshvara</a> (Alagakkonara) of Ceylon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[d]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-duv38-373_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-duv38-373-60">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-233-5_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-233-5-62">[58]</a></sup> Alakeshvara posed a threat to the neighboring countries and local waters of Ceylon and southern India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-70-3_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-d7-70-3-63">[59]</a></sup> On arrival at Ceylon, the Chinese were overbearing and contemptuous of the Sinhalese, whom they considered rude, disrespectful, and hostile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-67-8_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-d7-67-8-64">[60]</a></sup> They also resented that the Sinhalese were committing attacks and piracy towards neighboring countries who had diplomatic relations with Ming China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-67-8_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-d7-67-8-64">[60]</a></sup> Admiral Zheng He and a few of his troops traveled overland into Kotte, because Alakeshvara had lured them into his territory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-67-8_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-d7-67-8-64">[60]</a></sup> Alakeshvara cut off Admiral Zheng He and his 2000 accompanying troops from the treasure fleet anchored at Colombo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7678703_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-drey7678703-65">[61]</a></sup> He also planned to launch an attack on the fleet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-233_24-17"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-233-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-70-3_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-d7-70-3-63">[59]</a></sup> In response, Admiral Zheng He and his troops <a href="/wiki/Ming%E2%80%93Kotte_War" title="Ming&#8211;Kotte War">invaded Kotte, conquering its capital</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-70-3_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-d7-70-3-63">[59]</a></sup> They took captive Alakeshvara, his family, and principal officials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-11-2_58-5"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-11-2-58">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7678703_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-drey7678703-65">[61]</a></sup> The Sinhalese army hastily returned and surrounded the capital, but they were repeatedly defeated in battle against the invading Chinese troops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-67-8_64-3"><a href="#cite_note-d7-67-8-64">[60]</a></sup> The opposing Sinhalese army was said to have over 50,000 troops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-70-3_63-3"><a href="#cite_note-d7-70-3-63">[59]</a></sup> The king and his family were taken captive to Nanjing, China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-233-5_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-233-5-62">[58]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Admiral Zheng He returned to Nanjing on 6 July 1411.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-11-2_58-6"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-11-2-58">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[62]</a></sup> He presented the Sinhalese captives to the Yongle Emperor,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-11-2_58-7"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-11-2-58">[55]</a></sup> who decided to free and return them to their country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-11-2_58-8"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-11-2-58">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-233-5_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-233-5-62">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-70-3_63-4"><a href="#cite_note-d7-70-3-63">[59]</a></sup> The Chinese dethroned Alakeshvara in favor of their ally <a href="/wiki/Parakramabahu_VI_of_Kotte" title="Parakramabahu VI of Kotte">Parakramabahu VI</a> as the king with the backing of Zheng He and his fleet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[64]</a></sup> From then on, the treasure fleet would experience no hostilities during visits to Ceylon on subsequent treasure voyages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-70-3_63-5"><a href="#cite_note-d7-70-3-63">[59]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fourth_voyage">Fourth voyage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ming_treasure_voyages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Fourth voyage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Ming_Chinese_empire_1415.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2339" data-file-width="1577" decoding="async" height="326" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Map_of_Ming_Chinese_empire_1415.jpg/220px-Map_of_Ming_Chinese_empire_1415.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Map_of_Ming_Chinese_empire_1415.jpg/330px-Map_of_Ming_Chinese_empire_1415.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Map_of_Ming_Chinese_empire_1415.jpg/440px-Map_of_Ming_Chinese_empire_1415.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_Ming_Chinese_empire_1415.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ming China during the Yongle reign  (r. 1402&#8211;24)</div></div></div>\n<p>On 18 December 1412, the Yongle Emperor issued the order for the fourth voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-duv38-373_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-duv38-373-60">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dre7-75_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-dre7-75-69">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-12-3_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-12-3-70">[66]</a></sup> Admiral Zheng He and others were commanded to lead it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dre7-75_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-dre7-75-69">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-12-3_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-12-3-70">[66]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Yongle Emperor attended an archery contest for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Duanwu_Festival" title="Duanwu Festival">Midsummer Festival</a> of 1413 (5th day, 5th month, 11th year).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-375_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-du38-375-71">[67]</a></sup> All the Chinese officials and "barbarian" envoys were invited to attend this event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-375_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-du38-375-71">[67]</a></sup> Duyvendak (1939) states that these envoys were so numerous that they most-likely comprised many of those whom Admiral Zheng He would escort back to their countries during the fourth voyage than just those from close neighbors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-375_71-2"><a href="#cite_note-du38-375-71">[67]</a></sup> This expedition would lead the treasure fleet into Muslim countries, so it must have been important for the Chinese to seek out reliable interpreters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-375-6_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-du38-375-6-72">[68]</a></sup> The interpreter Ma Huan joined the voyages for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-375-6_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-du38-375-6-72">[68]</a></sup> A 1523 inscription at a Muslim mosque in <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi\'an">Xi\'an</a> recorded that, on the 4th month of the 11th year, Admiral Zheng He was there to seek reliable interpreters and found a man named Hasan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-375-6_72-2"><a href="#cite_note-du38-375-6-72">[68]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Admiral Zheng He\'s fleet left Nanjing in 1413, probably in the autumn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-12-3_70-2"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-12-3-70">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-76_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-76-73">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-374&amp;6_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-du38-374&amp;6-74">[70]</a></sup> They set sail from Fujian in the 12th month of the 11th year in the Yongle reign (23 December 1413 to 21 January 1414).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-76_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-76-73">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-374&amp;6_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-du38-374&amp;6-74">[70]</a></sup> Calicut was the westernmost destination during the previous voyages, but now the fleet sailed to lands further away.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-77_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-77-75">[71]</a></sup> The <i>Taizong Shilu</i> recorded Malacca, Java, Champa, Semudera, Aru, Cochin, Calicut, Lambri, Pahang, Kelantan, <i>Jiayile</i>, Ormuz, <i>Bila</i>, Maldives, and <i>Sunla</i> for this voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-duv38-373_60-3"><a href="#cite_note-duv38-373-60">[57]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The fleet sailed to Champa,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-76_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-76-73">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-235_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-235-76">[72]</a></sup> Kelatan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-235_76-1"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-235-76">[72]</a></sup> Pahang,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-235_76-2"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-235-76">[72]</a></sup> Malacca,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-76_73-3"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-76-73">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-235_76-3"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-235-76">[72]</a></sup> Palembang,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-235_76-4"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-235-76">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[73]</a></sup> Java,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-77_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-77-75">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-235_76-5"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-235-76">[72]</a></sup> Lambri,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-77_75-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-77-75">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-235_76-6"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-235-76">[72]</a></sup> Lide,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-77_75-3"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-77-75">[71]</a></sup> Aru,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-77_75-4"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-77-75">[71]</a></sup> Semudera,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-77_75-5"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-77-75">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-235_76-7"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-235-76">[72]</a></sup> Ceylon,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-77_75-6"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-77-75">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-235_76-8"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-235-76">[72]</a></sup> <i>Jiayile</i> (opposite Ceylon),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-77_75-7"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-77-75">[71]</a></sup> Cochin;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-77_75-8"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-77-75">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-235_76-9"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-235-76">[72]</a></sup> and Calicut.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-77_75-9"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-77-75">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-235_76-10"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-235-76">[72]</a></sup> They proceeded to <i>Liushan</i> (<a href="/wiki/Maldive_Islands" title="Maldive Islands">Maldive</a> and <a href="/wiki/Laccadive_Islands" title="Laccadive Islands">Laccadive Islands</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-235_76-11"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-235-76">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-77-8_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-77-8-78">[74]</a></sup> <i>Bila</i> (<a href="/wiki/Bitra" title="Bitra">Bitra Atoll</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-77-8_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-77-8-78">[74]</a></sup> <i>Sunla</i> (<a href="/wiki/Chetlat_Island" title="Chetlat Island">Chetlat Atoll</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-77-8_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-77-8-78">[74]</a></sup> and Hormuz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-235_76-12"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-235-76">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-77-8_78-3"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-77-8-78">[74]</a></sup> At Java, the fleet delivered gifts and favors from the Yongle Emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-77_75-10"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-77-75">[71]</a></sup> In return, a Javanese envoy arrived in China on 29 April 1415, presenting tribute in the form of "western horses" and local products while expressing gratitude.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-77_75-11"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-77-75">[71]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1415, the fleet made a stop at northern Sumatra during the journey homeward from Hormuz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-79-81_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-79-81-79">[75]</a></sup> They engaged Sekandar at this point of the voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-235_76-13"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-235-76">[72]</a></sup> Sekandar had usurped the Semudera throne from Zain al-\'Abidin,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-12-3_70-3"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-12-3-70">[66]</a></sup> but the Chinese had formally recognized the latter as the King of Semudera.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-79-81_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-79-81-79">[75]</a></sup> Even though Sekandar was an autonomous ruler, he was not recognized by the Chinese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-79-81_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-79-81-79">[75]</a></sup> Fei Xin described Sekandar as a false king who robbed, stole, and usurped the throne of Semudera, Ma Huan portrayed him as someone who attempted to overthrow the ruler, the <i>Ming Shilu</i> noted that Sekandar was the younger brother of the former king and plotted to kill the ruler.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-se-614-5_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-se-614-5-80">[76]</a></sup> Admiral Zheng He had orders to launch a punitive attack against the usurper and restore Zain al-\'Abidin as the rightful king.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-12-3_70-4"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-12-3-70">[66]</a></sup> In retaliation, Sekandar led his forces to attack the Ming forces and was defeated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-79-81_79-3"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-79-81-79">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-se-614-5_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-se-614-5-80">[76]</a></sup> He reportedly attacked with "tens of thousands" of soldiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-se-614-5_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-se-614-5-80">[76]</a></sup> The Ming forces pursued Sekandar\'s forces to Lambri where they caught Sekandar, his wife, and his child.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-12-3_70-5"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-12-3-70">[66]</a></sup> King Zain al-\'Abidin later dispatched a tribute mission to express his gratefulness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-79-81_79-4"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-79-81-79">[75]</a></sup> This conflict reaffirmed Chinese power over the foreign states and the maritime route by protecting the local political authority that sheltered the trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-79-81_79-5"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-79-81-79">[75]</a></sup> Sekandar was presented to the Yongle Emperor at the palace gate and later executed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-12-3_70-6"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-12-3-70">[66]</a></sup> It is not known when this execution happened, but Ma Huan stated that Sekandar was publicly executed in the capital after the fleet returned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-81_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-81-81">[77]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 12 August 1415, Admiral Zheng He\'s fleet returned to Nanjing from his voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-duv38-373_60-4"><a href="#cite_note-duv38-373-60">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-12-3_70-7"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-12-3-70">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-81_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-81-81">[77]</a></sup> The Yongle Emperor was away since 16 March 1413 for his <a href="/wiki/Yongle_Emperor%27s_campaigns_against_the_Mongols" title="Yongle Emperor\'s campaigns against the Mongols">second Mongol campaign</a> and hadn\'t returned when the fleet arrived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-76_73-4"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-76-73">[69]</a></sup> After the fleet\'s return, rulers of 18 countries sent envoys bearing tribute to the Ming court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-235_76-14"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-235-76">[72]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fifth_voyage">Fifth voyage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ming_treasure_voyages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Fifth voyage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>On 14 November 1416, the Yongle Emperor returned to Nanjing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dre7-82_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-dre7-82-82">[78]</a></sup> On 19 November, a grand ceremony was held where the Yongle Emperor bestowed gifts to princes, civil officials, military officers, and the ambassadors of 18 countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dre7-82_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-dre7-82-82">[78]</a></sup> On 19 December, the eighteen<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[e]</a></sup> ambassadors were received at the Ming court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-13_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-13-84">[79]</a></sup> On 28 December, they visited the Ming court to take their leave and were bestowed robes before their departure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dre7-82_82-2"><a href="#cite_note-dre7-82-82">[78]</a></sup> That day, the Yongle Emperor ordered the undertaking of the fifth voyage,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-76_73-5"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-76-73">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-13_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-13-84">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du28-378_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-du28-378-85">[80]</a></sup> which had the avowed objective to return the 18 ambassadors and to reward their kings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-13_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-13-84">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du28-378_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-du28-378-85">[80]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Admiral Zheng He and other unnamed people had received orders to escort the ambassadors back home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dre7-82_82-3"><a href="#cite_note-dre7-82-82">[78]</a></sup> They carried imperial letters and many gifts for several kings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dre7-82_82-4"><a href="#cite_note-dre7-82-82">[78]</a></sup> The King of Cochin received special treatment, because he had sent tribute since 1411 and later also sent ambassadors to request the patent of investiture and a seal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dre7-82_82-5"><a href="#cite_note-dre7-82-82">[78]</a></sup> The Yongle Emperor granted him both requests, conferred to him a long inscription (allegedly composed by the emperor himself), and gave the title "State Protecting Mountain" to a hill in Cochin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dre7-82_82-6"><a href="#cite_note-dre7-82-82">[78]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Admiral Zheng He may have left the Chinese coast in the autumn of 1417.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-13_84-3"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-13-84">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-83_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-83-86">[81]</a></sup> He first made port at Quanzhou to load up the fleet\'s cargoholds with <a href="/wiki/Porcelain" title="Porcelain">porcelain</a> and other goods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[82]</a></sup> Archaeological finds of contemporary Chinese porcelain have been excavated at the East African places visited by Zheng He\'s fleet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-84_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-84-88">[83]</a></sup> A Ming tablet at Quanzhou commemorates that Admiral Zheng He burned incense for divine protection for this voyage on 31 May 1417.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-84_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-84-88">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-381_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-du38-381-89">[84]</a></sup> The fleet visited Champa, Pahang, Java, Palembang, Malacca, Semudera, Lambri, Ceylon, Cochin, Calicut, <i>Shaliwanni</i> (possibly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cannanore" title="Cannanore">Cannanore</a>), <i>Liushan</i> (Maladive and Laccadive Islands), Hormuz, <i>Lasa</i>, <a href="/wiki/Aden" title="Aden">Aden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mogadishu" title="Mogadishu">Mogadishu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barawa" title="Barawa">Brava</a>, <i>Zhubu</i>, and <a href="/wiki/Malindi" title="Malindi">Malindi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[85]</a></sup> For Arabia and East Africa, the most-likely route was Hormuz, <i>Lasa</i>, Aden, Mogadishu, Brava, <i>Zhubu</i>, and then Malindi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-83-7-9_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-d7-83-7-9-91">[86]</a></sup> Duyvendak suggested that Zheng He may have made a show of military force at Mogadishu and <i>Lasa</i> due to the unwelcome reception by the locals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-13_84-4"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-13-84">[79]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 8 August 1419, the fleet had returned to China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-13_84-5"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-13-84">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-84_88-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-84-88">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du28-382_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-du28-382-92">[87]</a></sup> The Yongle Emperor was in Beijing at the time, but he ordered the Ministry of Rites to give monetary rewards to the fleet\'s personnel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dre7-91_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-dre7-91-93">[88]</a></sup> The accompanied ambassadors were received at the Ming court in the eighth lunar month (21 August to 19 September) of 1419.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-13_84-6"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-13-84">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du28-382_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-du28-382-92">[87]</a></sup> Their tribute included lions, leopards, dromedary camels, ostriches, zebras, rhinoceroses, antelopes, giraffes, and other exotic animals,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-235_76-15"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-235-76">[72]</a></sup> causing a great sensation among those at the Ming court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du28-382_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-du28-382-92">[87]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sixth_voyage">Sixth voyage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ming_treasure_voyages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Sixth voyage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <i>Taizong Shilu</i> 3 March 1421 entry noted that the envoys of sixteen countries (Hormuz and other countries) were given gifts of paper and coin money, and ceremonial robes and linings before returning to their respective countries under escort of the treasure fleet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dre7-91_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-dre7-91-93">[88]</a></sup> The imperial order for the sixth voyage was dated 3 March 1421.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-14_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-14-94">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du28-385_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-du28-385-95">[90]</a></sup> Admiral Zheng He was dispatched with imperial letters, silk brocade, silk floss, silk gauze, and other gifts for the rulers of these countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dre7-91_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-dre7-91-93">[88]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gong_Zhen" title="Gong Zhen">Gong Zhen</a>\'s <i>Xiyang Fanguo Zhi</i> recorded a 10 November 1421 imperial edict instructing <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">Zheng He</a>, Kong He (&#23380;&#21644;), Zhu Buhua (&#26417;&#21340;&#33457;), and Tang Guanbao (&#21776;&#35264;&#20445;) to arrange the provisions for <a href="/wiki/Hong_Bao" title="Hong Bao">Hong Bao</a> and others for their dispatch to escort foreign envoys home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du28-385_95-1"><a href="#cite_note-du28-385-95">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-57_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-57-96">[91]</a></sup> The envoys of the 16 different states were escorted to their homelands by the treasure fleet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-93_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-93-97">[92]</a></sup> It\'s likely that the first few destinations were Malacca and the three Sumatran states of Lambri, Aru, and Semudera.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-93_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-93-97">[92]</a></sup> The fleet was divided in several detached squadrons at Semudera.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-14_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-14-94">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-93_97-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-93-97">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch4-29_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-ch4-29-98">[93]</a></sup> All the squadrons proceeded to Ceylon, whereafter they separated for <i>Jiayile</i>, Cochin, <i>Ganbali</i>, or Calicut in southern India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-93_97-3"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-93-97">[92]</a></sup> The squadrons traveled from there to their respective destinations at <i>Liushan</i> (Maldive and Laccadive Islands), Hormuz at the Persian Gulf, the three Arabian states of Djofar, <i>Lasa</i>, and Aden, and the two African states of Mogadishu and Brava.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-93_97-4"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-93-97">[92]</a></sup> The eunuch Zhou (probably <a href="/wiki/Zhou_Man" title="Zhou Man">Zhou Man</a>) led the detached squadron to Aden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-14_94-2"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-14-94">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch4-29_98-1"><a href="#cite_note-ch4-29-98">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dre7-94_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-dre7-94-99">[94]</a></sup> Ma Huan mentions Zhou Man and Li Xing in connection to the visit of Aden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-146_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-d7-146-100">[95]</a></sup> Their squadron may have also visited <i>Lasa</i> and Djofar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dre7-94_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-dre7-94-99">[94]</a></sup> According to the <i>Mingshi</i>, Admiral Zheng He personally visited <i>Ganbali</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[f]</a></sup> as an envoy in 1421.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-93_97-5"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-93-97">[92]</a></sup> Of of the twelve visited nations west of Sumatra, this was the only one noted to have been visited by Admiral Zheng He himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-93_97-6"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-93-97">[92]</a></sup> Even though Quilon was not visited, the squadron for Mogadishu probably separated near Quilon as a navigation point while the main fleet continued to Calicut.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dre7-94_99-2"><a href="#cite_note-dre7-94-99">[94]</a></sup> A large squadron proceeded further from Calicut to Hormuz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dre7-94_99-3"><a href="#cite_note-dre7-94-99">[94]</a></sup> They may have traveled via the Laccadives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dre7-94_99-4"><a href="#cite_note-dre7-94-99">[94]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On the return, several squadrons regrouped at Calicut and all the squadrons regrouped further at Semudera.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dre7-94_99-5"><a href="#cite_note-dre7-94-99">[94]</a></sup> Siam was likely visited during the return journey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-93_97-7"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-93-97">[92]</a></sup> The fleet returned on 3 September 1422.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du28-385_95-2"><a href="#cite_note-du28-385-95">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-92-4_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-92-4-102">[96]</a></sup> They brought with them envoys from Siam, Semudera, Aden, and other countries, who bore tribute in local products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-92-4_102-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-92-4-102">[96]</a></sup> The foreign envoys, who traveled to China with the fleet, proceeded overland or via the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Canal_(China)" title="Grand Canal (China)">Grand Canal</a> before reaching the imperial court at Beijing in 1423.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-144_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-144-103">[97]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nanjing_garrison">Nanjing garrison</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ming_treasure_voyages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Nanjing garrison">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nanking_Erlach.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="927" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Nanking_Erlach.jpg/220px-Nanking_Erlach.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Nanking_Erlach.jpg/330px-Nanking_Erlach.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Nanking_Erlach.jpg/440px-Nanking_Erlach.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nanking_Erlach.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Bao%27en_Temple" title="Great Bao\'en Temple">Great Bao\'en Temple</a> as depicted in <a href="/wiki/Johann_Bernhard_Fischer_von_Erlach" title="Johann Bernhard Fischer von Erlach">Fischer von Erlach</a>\'s <i>A Plan of Civil and Historical Architecture</i> (1721)</div></div></div>\n<p>On 14 May 1421, the Yongle Emperor had ordered the temporary suspension of the voyages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[g]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-138_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-138-105">[98]</a></sup> At the expense of the treasure fleet\'s voyages, the imperial attention and funding was diverted for the <a href="/wiki/Yongle_Emperor%27s_campaigns_against_the_Mongols" title="Yongle Emperor\'s campaigns against the Mongols">third, fourth, and fifth Mongol campaigns</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[99]</a></sup> Between 1422 and 1431, the treasure fleet remained in Nanjing to serve in the city\'s garrison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-99_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-99-107">[100]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1424, Admiral Zheng He departed on a diplomatic mission to Palembang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[h]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ray87-162_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-ray87-162-110">[102]</a></sup> Meanwhile, Zhu Gaozhi inherited the throne as the <a href="/wiki/Hongxi_Emperor" title="Hongxi Emperor">Hongxi Emperor</a> on 7 September 1424 after the death of the Yongle Emperor on 12 August 1424.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-1367_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-1367-111">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-388_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-du38-388-112">[104]</a></sup> Zheng He\'s return from Palembang was after this death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ray87-162_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-ray87-162-110">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-387_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-du38-387-114">[106]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 7 September 1424, the Hongxi Emperor terminated the undertaking of further treasure voyages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-137_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-137-115">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[108]</a></sup> He was hostile to the undertaking of the voyages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dre7-91_93-3"><a href="#cite_note-dre7-91-93">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[109]</a></sup> Nevertheless, he kept the treasure fleet as a part of Nanjing\'s garrison.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-167_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-167-118">[110]</a></sup> The fleet also retained its original designation "Xiafan Guanjun" (&#19979;&#30058;&#23448;&#36557;; lit. "foreign expeditionary armada").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-167_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-167-118">[110]</a></sup> On 24 February 1425, he appointed Admiral Zheng He as the defender of Nanjing and ordered him to continue his command over the treasure fleet for the city\'s defense.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-13940_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-13940-119">[111]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 25 March 1428, the Xuande Emperor ordered Zheng He and others to takeover the supervision for the rebuilding and repair of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Bao%27en_Temple" title="Great Bao\'en Temple">Great Bao\'en Temple</a> at Nanjing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[112]</a></sup> He completed the construction of the temple in 1431.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[113]</a></sup> It\'s speculated that the funds for the construction of the Great Bao\'en Temple was diverted from those for the treasure voyages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[114]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Seventh_voyage">Seventh voyage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ming_treasure_voyages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Seventh voyage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zheng-He-7th-expedition-map.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="837" data-file-width="1100" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Zheng-He-7th-expedition-map.svg/220px-Zheng-He-7th-expedition-map.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Zheng-He-7th-expedition-map.svg/330px-Zheng-He-7th-expedition-map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Zheng-He-7th-expedition-map.svg/440px-Zheng-He-7th-expedition-map.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zheng-He-7th-expedition-map.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Route of the seventh voyage</div></div></div>\n<p>Gong Zhen recorded that an imperial order was issued on 25 May 1430 for the arrangement of necessary provisions for the dispatch of Zheng He, Wang Jinghong, Li Xing, Zhu Liang, Yang Zhen, Hong Bao, and others on official business to the countries of the Western Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-57_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-57-96">[91]</a></sup> It was addressed to Yang Qing (&#26954;&#24950;), Luo Zhi (&#32645;&#26234;), Tang Guanbo (&#21776;&#35264;&#20445;), and Yuan Cheng (&#34945;&#35488;).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-57_96-2"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-57-96">[91]</a></sup> On 29 June 1430, the Xuande Emperor issued his orders for the seventh voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-14_94-3"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-14-94">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-144_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-144-103">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-390_123-0"><a href="#cite_note-du38-390-123">[115]</a></sup> It was addressed to Zheng He and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-14_94-4"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-14-94">[89]</a></sup> The <i>Xuanzong Shilu</i> reported that Zheng He, <a href="/wiki/Wang_Jinghong" title="Wang Jinghong">Wang
