Jinghong</a>, and others were sent to far-lying foreign lands to bring them into deference and submission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-144_103-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-144-103">[97]</a></sup> The emperor wished to reinvigorate the tributary relations that had been promoted during the Yongle reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch8-302_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-ch8-302-124">[116]</a></sup> Before departing for the seventh voyage, Admiral Zheng He and his associates had the Liujiagang and Changle inscriptions inscribed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-135_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-135-125">[117]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <i>Xia Xiyang</i> provides valuable information, as described hereafter, about the dates and itinerary for this voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-xiaxiyang-7th_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-xiaxiyang-7th-126">[i]</a></sup> The fleet embarked from Longwan (lit. "dragon bay") in Nanjing on 19 January 1431.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-151_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-151-127">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-15_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-15-128">[119]</a></sup> On 23 January, the fleet made a stop at Xushan, a currently-unknown island in the Yangtze, where the crew hunted animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-151_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-151-127">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-15_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-15-128">[119]</a></sup> On 2 February 1431, the fleet sailed through the Fuzi Passage (present-day Baimaosha Channel) to the estuary Yangtze River before arriving at Liujiagang the following day (3 February).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-151_127-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-151-127">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-15_128-2"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-15-128">[119]</a></sup> On 14 March 1431, the Liujiagang inscription was erected there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-145-50_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-d7-145-50-129">[120]</a></sup> On 8 April 1431, the fleet arrived at Changle, where they remained until mid-December.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-151_127-3"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-151-127">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-15_128-3"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-15-128">[119]</a></sup> The Changle inscription, dated to the 11th month of the 6th year of the Xuande reign, was erected during the end of their stay there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-145-50_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-d7-145-50-129">[120]</a></sup> On 16 December, they traveled to the Fu Tou Shan, possibly near Fuzhou.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-15_128-4"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-15-128">[119]</a></sup> The treasure fleet sailed through the Wuhumen on 12 January 1431.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-151_127-4"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-151-127">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-15_128-5"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-15-128">[119]</a></sup> On 27 January 1432, the fleet made a call at the capital city <a href="/wiki/Vijaya_(Champa)" title="Vijaya (Champa)">Vijaya</a> (near present-day <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qui_Nhon" title="Qui Nhon">Qui Nhon</a>) of Champa before departing on 12 February.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-15_128-6"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-15-128">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[121]</a></sup> On 7 March 1432, the fleet arrived at Java, where they made port at <a href="/wiki/Surabaya" title="Surabaya">Surabaya</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-15_128-7"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-15-128">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-152_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-152-131">[122]</a></sup> The fleet remained in the region before departing on 13 July.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-15_128-8"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-15-128">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-152_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-152-131">[122]</a></sup> On 24 July 1432, the fleet arrived at Palembang before departing on 27 July.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-152_131-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-152-131">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-17_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-17-132">[123]</a></sup> From Palembang, the fleet sailed down the <a href="/wiki/Musi_River_(Indonesia)" title="Musi River (Indonesia)">Musi River</a>, through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banka_Strait" title="Banka Strait">Banka Strait</a>, passing the Lingga and Riau archipelagos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-153_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-153-133">[124]</a></sup> The Lingga and Riau archipelagos had a considerable pirate population that posed a threat to passing ships, but these pirates posed no threat to the treasure fleet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-153_133-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-153-133">[124]</a></sup> On 3 August 1432, the treasure fleet arrived at Malacca.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-17_132-1"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-17-132">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-153_133-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-153-133">[124]</a></sup> The fleet departed from Malacca on 2 September 1432.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-17_132-2"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-17-132">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-153_133-3"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-153-133">[124]</a></sup> They traveled to Semudera and arrived there on 12 September.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-17_132-3"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-17-132">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-153_133-4"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-153-133">[124]</a></sup> On 2 November 1432, the fleet departed from Semudera.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-17_132-4"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-17-132">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-153_133-5"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-153-133">[124]</a></sup> On 28 November 1432, the fleet arrived at Beruwala on Ceylon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-17_132-5"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-17-132">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-153_133-6"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-153-133">[124]</a></sup> The fleet departed from Beruwala on 2 December.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-17_132-6"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-17-132">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-154_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-154-134">[125]</a></sup> They arrived at Calicut on 10 December.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-17_132-7"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-17-132">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-154_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-154-134">[125]</a></sup> The fleet departed from Calicut to Hormuz on 14 December 1432.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-17_132-8"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-17-132">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-155_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-155-135">[126]</a></sup> They arrived at Hormuz on 17 January 1433.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-17_132-9"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-17-132">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-155_135-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-155-135">[126]</a></sup>  The treasure fleet remained in Hormuz for almost two months before traveling homeward on 9 March 1433.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-17_132-10"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-17-132">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-155_135-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-155-135">[126]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:350px; border-width: 1px; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303512" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>\n<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">We have traversed more than one hundred thousand <i>li</i> of immense water spaces, and have beheld in the ocean huge waves like mountains rising sky-high. We have set eyes on barbarian regions far away hidden in a blue transparency of light vapours, while our sails, loftily unfurled like clouds, day and night continued their course with starry speed, breasting the savage waves as if we were treading a public thoroughfare.</div>\n<p><cite class="right-aligned" style="">&#8212; <i>Admiral Zheng He and his associates <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nee59-558_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-nee59-558-136">[127]</a></sup></i></cite>\n</p>\n</div>\n<p>Of the eight destinations recorded for the seventh voyage in the <i>Xia Xiyang</i>, Hormuz was the westernmost place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-155_135-3"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-155-135">[126]</a></sup> The <i>Mingshi</i> and other sources described the voyage with at least a total of 17 visited countries (including those already mentioned in the <i>Xia Xiyang</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-155_135-4"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-155-135">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-18_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-18-137">[128]</a></sup> The additional destinations recorded in the <i>Mingshi</i> are Coimbator (<i>Ganbali</i>), Bengal, Laccadive and Maldive island chains, Djofar, <i>Lasa</i>, Aden, Mecca, Mogadishu, and Brava.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-156_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-156-138">[129]</a></sup> Gong Zhen even recorded a total of 20 visited countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-18_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-18-137">[128]</a></sup> During the voyage, as Fei Xin mentioned, the fleet made a stop at the Andaman and Nicobar island chains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-156_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-156-138">[129]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-18-9_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-18-9-139">[130]</a></sup> He wrote that, on 14 November 1432, the fleet arrived at <i>Cuilanxu</i> (probably the <a href="/wiki/Great_Nicobar_Island" title="Great Nicobar Island">Great Nicobar Island</a>) where they anchored for three days due to the unfavorable winds and waves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-154_134-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-154-134">[125]</a></sup> He further wrote that the native men and women came in log boats to trade coconuts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-154_134-3"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-154-134">[125]</a></sup> The neighboring Aru, Nagur, Lide, and Lambri were certainly visited by a few ships, according to Dreyer (2007), on the fleet\'s way to Semudera in northern Sumatra.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-156_138-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-156-138">[129]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Admiral Zheng He is mentioned in the <i>Mingshi</i> in connection to the visits of <i>Ganbali</i> (possibly Coimatore),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-155_135-5"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-155-135">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-19_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-19-140">[131]</a></sup> <i>Lasa</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-155_135-6"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-155-135">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-19_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-19-140">[131]</a></sup> Djorfar,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-155_135-7"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-155-135">[126]</a></sup> Magadishu,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-155_135-8"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-155-135">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-19_140-2"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-19-140">[131]</a></sup> and Brava.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-155_135-9"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-155-135">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-19_140-3"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-19-140">[131]</a></sup> Dreyer (2007) states that the account is unclear on whether he did go to those places in person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-155_135-10"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-155-135">[126]</a></sup> The wording in the <i>Mingshi</i> could indicate that he did as the account stated that he proclaimed imperial instructions to the kings of these countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-155_135-11"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-155-135">[126]</a></sup> Although, this may as well not be the case, because the fleet only made short stops at Calicut (4 days outward and 9 days homeward) that could not have provided enough time to travel overland to <i>Ganbali</i>, unless the location did not refer to Coimatore but elsewhere in southern India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[132]</a></sup> The overland journey may have been undertaken by someone else than Zheng He himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-156_138-3"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-156-138">[129]</a></sup> The <i>Mingshi</i> account about <i>Lasa</i> states that ambassadors from <i>Lasa</i>, Aden, and Brava traveled with Zheng He to China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-156_138-4"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-156-138">[129]</a></sup> This meant that the ships carrying them had possibly reassembled with the main fleet in Calicut according to Dreyer (2007), depending on whether Zheng He visited these countries in person or remained with the main portion of the fleet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-156_138-5"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-156-138">[129]</a></sup> Dreyer (2007) thinks that the detached squadrons had probably already assembled at Calicut for its homeward journey, because the main fleet didn\'t stay there for long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-160_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-160-142">[133]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Hong_Bao" title="Hong Bao">Hong Bao</a> commanded a squadron for the journey to Bengal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-156_138-6"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-156-138">[129]</a></sup> Ma Huan traveled with Hong Bao in this squadron.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-156-7_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-156-7-143">[134]</a></sup> It is not known when they exactly detached from the treasure fleet for Bengal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[j]</a></sup> They sailed from Semudera straight to Bengal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-157_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-157-145">[135]</a></sup> At Bengal, they traveled to Chittagong, then to Sonargaon, and finally to the capital Gaur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-35_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-35-146">[136]</a></sup> They then proceeded to sail from Bengal straight to Calicut.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-157_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-157-145">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-35_146-1"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-35-146">[136]</a></sup> Admiral Zheng He\'s fleet had departed from Calicut for Hormuz by the time Hong Bao\'s squadron arrived in Calicut.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-157_145-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-157-145">[135]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Once in Calicut, noticing that local ships were being prepared for Mecca, Hong Bao sent seven Chinese men to accompany a ship bound for Mecca.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-158-9_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-158-9-147">[137]</a></sup> It is likely<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[k]</a></sup> that one of the seven men was Ma Huan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-21_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-21-149">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-33_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-d7-33-150">[139]</a></sup> Ma Huan wrote about Mecca in the chapter <i>Tianfang</i> ("Heavenly Cube"), a reference to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qa%27aba" title="Qa\'aba">Qa\'aba</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-158-9_147-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-158-9-147">[137]</a></sup> After a year, the seven men returned with commodities and valuables that they had purchased, which included giraffes, lions, and ostriches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-158-9_147-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-158-9-147">[137]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dreyer (2007) suggests that Hong Bao may also have been involved with several other destinations, such as Djofar, <i>Lasa</i>, Aden, Mogadishu, and Brava.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[140]</a></sup> However, P. Pelliot suggests that the squadrons detached from the fleet at Hormuz to travel to Aden, the East African ports and perhaps <i>Lasa</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-21_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-21-149">[138]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dreyer (2007) states that the following countries may also have been visited by a few of the ships when the fleet passed by them: Siam; the northern Sumatran states of Aru, Nagur, Lide, and Lambri (when sailing to Semudera); Quilon and Cochin (when sailing to Calicut).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-156_138-7"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-156-138">[129]</a></sup> Mills (1970) concluded that Zheng He\'s associates and not Admiral Zheng He himself had visited Siam, Aru, Nagur, Lide, Lambri, Nicobar Islands, Bengal, Quilon, Cochi, Coimbatore, Maldive Islands, Dhufar, <i>Lasa</i>, Aden, Mecca, Mogadishu, and Brava.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-18-9_139-1"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-18-9-139">[130]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <i>Xia Xiyang</i> also provided the dates and itinerary, as described hereafter, for the return route of the seventh voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-xiaxiyang-7th_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-xiaxiyang-7th-126">[i]</a></sup> The fleet at Hormuz departed on 9 March 1433 and arrived at Calicut on 31 March.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-160_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-160-142">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-17-8_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-17-8-152">[141]</a></sup> On 9 April, the fleet departed from Calicut.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-160_142-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-160-142">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-17-8_152-1"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-17-8-152">[141]</a></sup> They sailed straight through the open ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-160_142-3"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-160-142">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-17-8_152-2"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-17-8-152">[141]</a></sup> On 25 April, the fleet arrived at Semudera.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-160_142-4"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-160-142">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-17-8_152-3"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-17-8-152">[141]</a></sup>  On 1 May, the fleet departed from Semudera.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-160_142-5"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-160-142">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-17-8_152-4"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-17-8-152">[141]</a></sup> On 9 May, the fleet arrived at Malacca.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-160_142-6"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-160-142">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-17-8_152-5"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-17-8-152">[141]</a></sup> The fleet arrived at the Kunlun Ocean<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[l]</a></sup> on 28 May 1433.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[m]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-17-8_152-6"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-17-8-152">[141]</a></sup> On 13 June, the fleet arrived at Vijaya (present-day Qiu Nhon).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-17-8_152-7"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-17-8-152">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-161_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-161-155">[142]</a></sup> The fleet departed from Vijaya on 17 June.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-17-8_152-8"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-17-8-152">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-161_155-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-161-155">[142]</a></sup>  The <i>Xia Xiyang</i> notes several geographical sightings<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[n]</a></sup> from now until the fleet entered Taicang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-161_155-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-161-155">[142]</a></sup> The fleet arrived at Taicang on 7 July 1433.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-17-8_152-9"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-17-8-152">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-161_155-3"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-161-155">[142]</a></sup> The <i>Xia Xiyang</i> notes that it had not recorded the journey from Taicang to the capital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-17-8_152-10"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-17-8-152">[141]</a></sup> On 22 July, they arrived in the capital Beijing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-17-8_152-11"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-17-8-152">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-1623_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-1623-157">[143]</a></sup> On 27 July, the Xuande Emperor bestowed ceremonial robes and paper money to the fleet\'s personnel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-17-8_152-12"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-17-8-152">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-1623_157-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-1623-157">[143]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Dreyer (2007) states that they did not make port at Ceylon or southern India, because they were sailing under favorable conditions and were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Running_(sailing)" title="Running (sailing)">running</a> before the southwest monsoon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-160_142-7"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-160-142">[133]</a></sup> Ma Huan recorded that the various detached ships reassembled in Malacca to wait for favorable winds before continuing their return.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-21_149-2"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-21-149">[138]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Admiral Zheng He came back with envoys from 11 countries, including one from Mecca.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-4_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-4-158">[144]</a></sup> On 14 September 1433, as recorded in the <i>Xuanzong Shilu</i>, the following envoys came to court to present tribute: King Zain al-Abidin of Semudera sent his younger brother <i>Halizhi Han</i> and others, King <i>Bilima</i> of Calicut sent his ambassador <i>Gebumanduluya</i> and others, King <i>Keyili</i> of Cochin sent his ambassador <i>Jiabubilima</i> and others, <a href="/wiki/Parakramabahu_VI_of_Kotte" title="Parakramabahu VI of Kotte">King Parakramabahu VI</a> of Ceylon sent his ambassador <i>Mennidenai</i> and others, King Ali of Djofar sent his ambassador Hajji Hussein and others, King Al-Malik az-Zahir Yahya b. Isma\'il of Aden sent his ambassador <i>Puba</i> and others, King Devaraja of Coimbatore sent his ambassador <i>Duansilijian</i> and others, King Sa\'if-ud-Din of Hormuz sent the foreigner <i>Malazu</i>, the King of "Old Kayal" (<i>Jiayile</i>) sent his ambassador Abd-ur-Rahman and others, and the King of Mecca sent the headman (<i>toumu</i>) <i>Shaxian</i> and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-1623_157-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-1623-157">[143]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aftermath">Aftermath</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ming_treasure_voyages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Aftermath">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zhenghemu03.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Zhenghemu03.jpg/220px-Zhenghemu03.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Zhenghemu03.jpg/330px-Zhenghemu03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Zhenghemu03.jpg/440px-Zhenghemu03.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zhenghemu03.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Admiral <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">Zheng He</a>\'s empty tomb at <a href="/wiki/Nanjing" title="Nanjing">Nanjing</a></div></div></div>\n<p>During the course of the voyages, Ming China had become the <a href="/wiki/Naval_history_of_China" title="Naval history of China">pre-eminent naval power</a> of the early 15th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lee10-95_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-lee10-95-159">[145]</a></sup> The Yongle Emperor had extended imperial control over foreign lands during the span of the voyages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fin8-336_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-fin8-336-160">[146]</a></sup> However, in 1433, the voyages ceased and Ming China turned away from the seas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fin8-338_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-fin8-338-161">[147]</a></sup>\n</p><p>It is not exactly known why the voyages completely ended in 1433.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fair49-140_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-fair49-140-162">[148]</a></sup> Duyvendak (1939) suggested that the cessation of the expeditions was partly due to the considerable expenses,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[149]</a></sup> but Ray (1987), Finlay (1992), and Dreyer (1997) noted that the costs for undertaking the voyages had not overburdened the Ming treasury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ray87-165-7_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-ray87-165-7-164">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finl92-229_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-finl92-229-165">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-122_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-122-166">[152]</a></sup> Ray (1987) adds that the Ming treasure voyages were a profitable enterprise, rejecting the notion that the voyages were terminated because they were wasteful, costly, or uneconomic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ray87-165-7_164-1"><a href="#cite_note-ray87-165-7-164">[150]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Even though civil officials did have ill feelings towards eunuchs for their overbearing nature and interference in state affairs, much of the hostility that came to characterize the relationship between the officials and eunuchs manifested at a time, long after the termination of the voyages, when eunuchs wielded their power to enrich themselves through extortion and persecute their critics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lo-eun-off_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-lo-eun-off-167">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r87b-165_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-r87b-165-168">[154]</a></sup> As such, according to Lo (1958) and Ray (1987), the hostility between these factions can not explain the cessation of the voyages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lo-eun-off_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-lo-eun-off-167">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r87b-165_168-1"><a href="#cite_note-r87b-165-168">[154]</a></sup> Furthermore, Lo (1958) notes that Admiral Zheng He was on friendly terms with many high officials and was respected by them,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lo-eun-off_167-2"><a href="#cite_note-lo-eun-off-167">[153]</a></sup> while Ray (1987) mentions that eunuchs such as Zheng He and Hou Xian were held in high esteem by the court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r87b-165_168-2"><a href="#cite_note-r87b-165-168">[154]</a></sup> The voyages were also favorably depicted in contemporary records.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lo-eun-off_167-3"><a href="#cite_note-lo-eun-off-167">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r87b-165_168-3"><a href="#cite_note-r87b-165-168">[154]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The trade was still flourishing long after the voyages had ceased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fair49-141_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-fair49-141-169">[155]</a></sup> Chinese ships continued to control the Eastern Asian maritime trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-4_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-4-158">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lee10-96_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-lee10-96-170">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lo-prtr_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-lo-prtr-171">[157]</a></sup> They also continued trading around India and East Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lee10-96_170-1"><a href="#cite_note-lee10-96-170">[156]</a></sup> However, the imperial tributary system over the foreign regions and state monopoly over the foreign trade gradually broke down as time progressed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev6-174-5_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-lev6-174-5-172">[158]</a></sup> while private trade rose to supplant the commerce under the state\'s tributary system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lo-prtr_171-1"><a href="#cite_note-lo-prtr-171">[157]</a></sup> In addition, the foreign commerce shifted to the domain of local authorities, which further undermined the authority of the central government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev6-174-5_172-1"><a href="#cite_note-lev6-174-5-172">[158]</a></sup> The Ming treasure voyages provided direct links between the Ming court and foreign tribute states, thereby outflanking private commerce as well as local civil officials who tried to sabotage the <a href="/wiki/Haijin" title="Haijin">imperial prohibitions</a> targeting overseas exchange.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fin8-336_160-1"><a href="#cite_note-fin8-336-160">[146]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ray (1987) states that the cessation of the Ming treasure voyages primarily happened as traders and bureaucrats, for reasons of economic self-interest, gradually collapsed the framework supporting the state-controlled maritime enterprise and the strict regulation of the private commerce that was subjected to prohibitive policies through their connections in Beijing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[159]</a></sup> Similarly, Lo (1958) states that rich and influential individuals, who tried to safeguard their interests and were thus antagonistic to the government\'s monopoly of foreign trade, undermined efforts to restore the trade to official channels and thus possibly the revival of the voyages through their connections in Beijing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lo-prtr_171-2"><a href="#cite_note-lo-prtr-171">[157]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The nobility and military were an important part of the ruling elite during the Hongwu and Yongle reigns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-169_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-169-174">[160]</a></sup> Over time, the political power gradually shifted away from both the nobility and military to the civil officials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-169_174-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-169-174">[160]</a></sup> As a consequence, the eunuch faction were unable to gather enough support to initiate projects opposed by the civil government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-169_174-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-169-174">[160]</a></sup> Civil officials remained wary of future attempts of the eunuchs to reinitiate the treasure voyages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-169_174-3"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-169-174">[160]</a></sup> Moreover, no later emperor would seriously consider undertaking new expeditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[161]</a></sup> The withdrawal of Ming China\'s treasure fleet left an enormous void in the dominance over the Indian Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finl92-230_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-finl92-230-176">[162]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Impact">Impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ming_treasure_voyages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Goals_and_consequences">Goals and consequences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ming_treasure_voyages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Goals and consequences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The voyages were diplomatic, militaristic, and commercial in nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chu8-2354_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-chu8-2354-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[163]</a></sup> They were conducted to establish imperial control over the maritime trade,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mil70-1_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-mil70-1-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lee10-95_159-1"><a href="#cite_note-lee10-95-159">[145]</a></sup> to bring the maritime trade into the tributary system,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fai42-143_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-fai42-143-178">[164]</a></sup> and to force foreign countries into compliance within the tributary system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lee10-95_159-2"><a href="#cite_note-lee10-95-159">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fai42-143_178-1"><a href="#cite_note-fai42-143-178">[164]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[165]</a></sup> The diplomatic aspect comprised the announcement of the Yongle Emperor\'s accession to the throne, the establishment of hegemony over the foreign countries, and providing safe passage to foreign envoys who came bearing tribute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[166]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Chinese didn\'t seek territorial control, as they were primarily motivated by the political and economic control across space entailing a domination over a vast network with its ports and shipping lanes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sen16-631-3_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-sen16-631-3-181">[167]</a></sup> Finlay (2008) underscores the goal of controlling maritime commerce in which the Ming treasure voyages are regarded as an attempt to reconcile China\'s need for maritime commerce with the government\'s suppression of the private aspects, representing "a deployment of state power to bring into line the reality of seaborne commerce with an expansive conception of Chinese hegemony."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fin8-336_160-2"><a href="#cite_note-fin8-336-160">[146]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The voyages had a significant and lasting effect on the organization of a maritime network, utilizing and creating nodes and conduits in its wake, thereby restructuring international relationships and exchanges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sen16-609-11-631-3_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-sen16-609-11-631-3-182">[168]</a></sup> It was especially of note as no other polity had exerted naval dominance over all sectors of the Indian Ocean prior to the these voyages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[169]</a></sup> The Ming promoted alternative nodes as a strategy to establish control over the network.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-se16-615_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-se16-615-184">[170]</a></sup> For instance, due to Chinese involvement, ports such as <a href="/wiki/Malacca" title="Malacca">Malacca</a> (in Southeast Asia), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cochin" title="Cochin">Cochin</a> (on the Malabar Coast), and <a href="/wiki/Malindi" title="Malindi">Malindi</a> (on the Swahili Coast) had grown as key alternatives to other important and established ports.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[o]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[171]</a></sup> Through the voyages, Ming China intervened with the local affairs of foreign states and asserted itself in foreign lands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sen16-609-11-631-3_182-1"><a href="#cite_note-sen16-609-11-631-3-182">[168]</a></sup> The Chinese had installed or supported friendly local regimes, captured or executed rivals of local authorities, and threatened hostile local rulers into compliance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sen16-612-15_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-sen16-612-15-187">[172]</a></sup> The appearance of the Ming treasure fleet generated and intensified competition among contending polities and rivals, each seeking an alliance with the Ming.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sen16-612-15_187-1"><a href="#cite_note-sen16-612-15-187">[172]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The voyages had a lasting impact that led to the Western Ocean\'s <a href="/wiki/Regional_integration" title="Regional integration">regional integration</a> and the increase in <a href="/wiki/Mobilities" title="Mobilities">international circulation</a> of people, ideas, and goods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sen16-609-11-631-3_182-2"><a href="#cite_note-sen16-609-11-631-3-182">[168]</a></sup> It also provided a platform for cosmopolitan discourses, which took place in locations such as the ships of the Ming treasure fleet, the Ming capitals of Nanjing as well as Beijing, and the banquet receptions organized by the Ming court for foreign representatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sen16-609-11-631-3_182-3"><a href="#cite_note-sen16-609-11-631-3-182">[168]</a></sup> Diverse groups of people from across the maritime countries congregated, interacted, and traveled together as the Ming treasure fleet sailed from and to Ming China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sen16-609-11-631-3_182-4"><a href="#cite_note-sen16-609-11-631-3-182">[168]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Chinese had the intention to civilize the so-called barbarian peoples by bringing them into formal submission within Ming China\'s greater world order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finl92-2356_188-0"><a href="#cite_note-finl92-2356-188">[173]</a></sup> Foreign rulers were forced to acknowledge the inherent moral and cultural superiority of China, an obligation that could be achieved by paying homage and presenting tribute before the Ming court.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finl92-2356_188-1"><a href="#cite_note-finl92-2356-188">[173]</a></sup> During the course of the voyages, the Yongle Emperor reasserted the political and cultural hegemony of Ming China over all others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mil70-1_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-mil70-1-6">[6]</a></sup> The cultural aspect of the voyages appears in the Liujiagang inscription, which noted that "those among the foreigners who were resisting the transforming influence (genghua) of Chinese culture and were disrespectful, we captured alive, and brigands who indulged in violence and plunder, we ex-terminated. Consequently the sea-route was purified and tranquillised and the natives were enabled quietly to pursue their avocations."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[174]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The treasure fleet was, as Mills (1970) characterized, "an instrument of aggression and political dominance."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mil70-1_6-3"><a href="#cite_note-mil70-1-6">[6]</a></sup> It brought forth the manifestation of China\'s power and wealth to awe foreign lands under Chinese hegemony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-61_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-61-190">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[176]</a></sup> This was actualized by showing the Ming flag and establishing a military presence along the maritime trade routes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-79_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-79-192">[177]</a></sup> Diplomatic relationships came to be based on a mutually beneficial maritime commerce as well as a visible presence of a Chinese militaristic naval force in foreign waters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wang98-320-1_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-wang98-320-1-193">[178]</a></sup> The Ming Chinese naval superiority was namely a crucial factor in this interaction, as it was inadvisable to risk punitive action from the Chinese fleet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sen16-631-3_181-1"><a href="#cite_note-sen16-631-3-181">[167]</a></sup> In addition, it\'s also reasoned that the worthwhile and profitable nature of the Ming treasure voyages for foreign countries was a persuading factor to comply.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wang98-320-1_193-1"><a href="#cite_note-wang98-320-1-193">[178]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There is a theory, considered very unlikely, suggesting that the voyages was initiated to search for the dethroned <a href="/wiki/Jianwen_Emperor" title="Jianwen Emperor">Jianwen Emperor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-60-1_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-60-1-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chu8-2354_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-chu8-2354-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-232_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-232-194">[179]</a></sup> This search is mentioned as a reason for the voyages in the later <i>Mingshi</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chu8-2354_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-chu8-2354-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[180]</a></sup> As the Yongle Emperor had usurped the throne, it actually may have been that he sought to legitimize his reign by forcing the foreign countries to recognize their tributary status within Ming China\'s greater world order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wang98-320-1_193-2"><a href="#cite_note-wang98-320-1-193">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-168_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-168-196">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[182]</a></sup> To this end, according to Wang (1998), the announced intention of the Yongle Emperor to find the deposed Jianwen Emperor may have served no more than a public justification for the voyages in face of the prohibitive policies for military actions overseas from the Hongwu reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wang98-320-1_193-3"><a href="#cite_note-wang98-320-1-193">[178]</a></sup> Another theory, also considered very unlikely, explained that the voyages were triggered in response to another power across Asia, namely the <a href="/wiki/Timurid_Empire" title="Timurid Empire">Timurid state</a> of <a href="/wiki/Timur" title="Timur">Tamerlane</a>, an enemy of Ming China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-60-1_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-60-1-9">[9]</a></sup> However, upon Tamerlane\'s death in 1405, Ming China was left unchallenged by the Timurid, because its new ruler <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shahrukh_Mirza" title="Shahrukh Mirza">Shahrukh</a> (r. 1405&#8211;1447) normalized diplomatic relations with China and was preoccupied with holding his state together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-60-1_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-60-1-9">[9]</a></sup> The Jianwen and Tamerlane factors are both absent from contemporary historical sources, so they lack the support and conformation to be accepted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-60-1_9-4"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-60-1-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Policy_and_administration">Policy and administration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ming_treasure_voyages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Policy and administration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Xuanzong_of_Ming.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2582" data-file-width="2022" decoding="async" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Xuanzong_of_Ming.jpg/220px-Xuanzong_of_Ming.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Xuanzong_of_Ming.jpg/330px-Xuanzong_of_Ming.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Xuanzong_of_Ming.jpg/440px-Xuanzong_of_Ming.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Xuanzong_of_Ming.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Painting of the <a href="/wiki/Xuande_Emperor" title="Xuande Emperor">Xuande Emperor</a> (<a href="/wiki/National_Palace_Museum" title="National Palace Museum">National Palace Museum</a>)</div></div></div>\n<p>Zheng He served as the Grand Director in the Directorate of Palace Servants, a eunuch-dominated department, before his appointment to command the expeditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-50_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-50-198">[183]</a></sup> Construction projects were usually the domain of eunuchs, who were often assigned to supervise them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-50_198-1"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-50-198">[183]</a></sup> The treasure fleet\'s construction was not different in that eunuchs were assigned to supervise it, while the military were assigned to carry it out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-4950_5-3"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-4950-5">[5]</a></sup> Civil officials criticized the state expenses brought by the fleet\'s construction, but the emperor was set to continue his grand plans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-4950_5-4"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-4950-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the Ming court, the civil officials were the faction who were against the voyages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finl92-229_165-1"><a href="#cite_note-finl92-229-165">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-168_196-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-168-196">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-398-9_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-du38-398-9-199">[184]</a></sup> In contrast, the eunuch establishment stood at the head of the fleet and the expeditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fin8-338_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-fin8-338-161">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fair49-140_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-fair49-140-162">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-168_196-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-168-196">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-398-9_199-1"><a href="#cite_note-du38-398-9-199">[184]</a></sup> The civil officials condemned the expeditions as extravagant and wasteful,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-398-9_199-2"><a href="#cite_note-du38-398-9-199">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch4-34-35_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-ch4-34-35-200">[185]</a></sup> but the Yongle Emperor was unconcerned about the costs of the voyages and was determined to undertake it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[186]</a></sup> Traditionally, the civil officials were political opponents of the eunuch faction,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fin8-338_161-2"><a href="#cite_note-fin8-338-161">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-168_196-3"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-168-196">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-398-9_199-3"><a href="#cite_note-du38-398-9-199">[184]</a></sup> but also to the military who crewed the fleet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-168_196-4"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-168-196">[181]</a></sup> This political and institutional disadvantage within the state system contributed to the inherent opposition of the civil officials against the voyages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-168_196-5"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-168-196">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-398-9_199-4"><a href="#cite_note-du38-398-9-199">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finl92-231_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-finl92-231-202">[187]</a></sup> On cultural grounds, the hostility came forth out of the trade and acquisition of strange foreign goods which conflicted with their Confucian ideologies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[188]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Finlay_2008_loc=341_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-Finlay_2008_loc=341-204">[189]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-398-9_199-5"><a href="#cite_note-du38-398-9-199">[184]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch4-34-35_200-1"><a href="#cite_note-ch4-34-35-200">[185]</a></sup> The undertaking of these expeditions only remained possible as long as the eunuchs maintained imperial favor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fair49-140_162-2"><a href="#cite_note-fair49-140-162">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-35_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-d7-35-205">[190]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Hongwu Emperor was wary of the political and social consequences that maritime commerce could bring,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[191]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[192]</a></sup> so he sought to restrain it by <a href="/wiki/Haijin" title="Haijin">outlawing private maritime trade</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-62_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-62-208">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fi8-335_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-fi8-335-209">[194]</a></sup> This policy continued well into the Yongle Emperor\'s reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-62_208-1"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-62-208">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fi8-335_209-1"><a href="#cite_note-fi8-335-209">[194]</a></sup> In addition, the Yongle Emperor aimed at consolidating imperial control over maritime commerce, stopping the coastal criminality and disorder, providing employment for mariners and entrepreneurs, exporting Chinese products to foreign markets, importing desired goods for Chinese consumers, extending the tributary system, and displaying imperial majesty to the seas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[195]</a></sup> The voyages functioned as trade commissions in the government\'s attempts to regulate maritime commerce by establishing an imperial monopoly over it and incorporating it into the tributary system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fin8-336_160-3"><a href="#cite_note-fin8-336-160">[146]</a></sup> There was seemingly an idea about a foreign policy comprising an extended foreign trade supported by a heavy military naval presence and a cultivation of shared interests with local allies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-61_190-1"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-61-190">[175]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The emperor\'s interest in the voyages was the highest during the period spanning the first three voyages, but he became more occupied with his <a href="/wiki/Yongle_Emperor%27s_campaigns_against_the_Mongols" title="Yongle Emperor\'s campaigns against the Mongols">offensive military campaigns against the Mongols</a> after establishing the capital at Beijing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-49_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-49-211">[196]</a></sup> By the fourth voyage, the emperor showed interest to expand the scope of trade and diplomatic activity to West Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r87a-78-9_212-0"><a href="#cite_note-r87a-78-9-212">[197]</a></sup> The Chinese therefore sought and employed interpreters of Persian or Arabic, such as Ma Huan and Guo Chongli, to accompany the fleet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r87a-78-9_212-1"><a href="#cite_note-r87a-78-9-212">[197]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the transfer of the capital from Nanjing to Beijing, the south and the seas were given less and less attention from emperors and officials alike.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-76_73-6"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-76-73">[69]</a></sup> The Hongxi Emperor wished to revert this predecessor\'s relocation of the capital, but he died on 29 May 1425 and thus this never took place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-13940_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-13940-119">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[198]</a></sup> He was succeeded by the Xuande Emperor who remained in Beijing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[199]</a></sup> In contrast to the Hongxi Emperor who relied on civil officials during his reign, the Xuande Emperor relied on eunuchs during his reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[200]</a></sup> Dreyer (2007) states that the prospects for the voyages would have been better if the capital was relocated back to Nanjing, because the court would have been near the Longjiang shipyards where most of the ships were built and the places where the voyages commenced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-169_174-4"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-169-174">[160]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Minister of Finance <a href="/wiki/Xia_Yuanji" title="Xia Yuanji">Xia Yuanji</a> (&#22799;&#21407;&#21513;) was a vocal opponent to the treasure voyages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-388_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-du38-388-112">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-xy_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-d7-xy-216">[201]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[202]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Hongxi_Emperor" title="Hongxi Emperor">Hongxi Emperor</a> was also fiercely against the treasure voyages throughout his reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dre7-91_93-4"><a href="#cite_note-dre7-91-93">[88]</a></sup> After the advice of Xia Yuanji, the emperor ordered the cessation of the treasure voyages on 7 September 1424, the day of his accession to the throne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-388_112-2"><a href="#cite_note-du38-388-112">[104]</a></sup> When the <a href="/wiki/Xuande_Emperor" title="Xuande Emperor">Xuande Emperor</a> ordered the seventh voyage, he went against the general court opinion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch8-302_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-ch8-302-124">[116]</a></sup> This was after the death of Xia Yuanji on 19 February 1430.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[203]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After 1433, the civil officials succeeded in halting subsequent maritime expeditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finl92-231_202-1"><a href="#cite_note-finl92-231-202">[187]</a></sup> The ships were left to rot, while their lumber was sold for fuel in Nanjing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finl92-231_202-2"><a href="#cite_note-finl92-231-202">[187]</a></sup> The mariners were reassigned to load grain on barges of the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Canal_(China)" title="Grand Canal (China)">Grand Canal</a> and to build the emperor\'s mausoleum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finl92-231_202-3"><a href="#cite_note-finl92-231-202">[187]</a></sup> After the voyages, subsequent Ming emperors would reject the Yongle Emperor\'s policy of bringing maritime trade into the structure of the tributary system.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fin8-338_161-3"><a href="#cite_note-fin8-338-161">[147]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personnel_and_organization">Personnel and organization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ming_treasure_voyages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Personnel and organization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zheng_He%27s_Treasure_Ship_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Zheng_He%27s_Treasure_Ship_1.jpg/220px-Zheng_He%27s_Treasure_Ship_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Zheng_He%27s_Treasure_Ship_1.jpg/330px-Zheng_He%27s_Treasure_Ship_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Zheng_He%27s_Treasure_Ship_1.jpg/440px-Zheng_He%27s_Treasure_Ship_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zheng_He%27s_Treasure_Ship_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Model of a <a href="/wiki/Chinese_treasure_ship" title="Chinese treasure ship">treasure ship</a> (<a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong_Science_Museum" title="Hong Kong Science Museum">Hong Kong Science Museum</a>)</div></div></div>\n<p>The fleet comprised an array of various ships, each likely fulfilling specialized functions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chu8-2355_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-chu8-2355-219">[204]</a></sup> Each treasure ship was crewed by about 500 men according to Mills (1970)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mil70-2_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-mil70-2-220">[205]</a></sup> or at least 600 men according to Finlay (1992).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finl92-227_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-finl92-227-221">[206]</a></sup> The high-ranking officers&#8212;Admiral Zheng He and his associates&#8212;were from the eunuch establishment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-102_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-102-222">[207]</a></sup> The majority of the crew came from the Ming military.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-102_222-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-102-222">[207]</a></sup> The crew was mostly recruited from Fujian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-232_194-1"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-232-194">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-391_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-du38-391-223">[208]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There were seven Grand Directors (<i>taijian</i>)&#8212;who served as the ambassadors and commanders of the fleet&#8212;followed by 10 Junior Directors (<i>shaojian</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-127_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-d7-127-224">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-31_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-31-225">[210]</a></sup> Admiral Zheng He was one of the Grand Directors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-127_224-1"><a href="#cite_note-d7-127-224">[209]</a></sup> Including these personnel who were all eunuchs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-31_225-1"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-31-225">[210]</a></sup> there was a total of 70 eunuchs in command of the treasure fleet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-127_224-2"><a href="#cite_note-d7-127-224">[209]</a></sup> This was followed by 2 brigadiers (<i>du zhihuishi</i>), 93 captains (<i>zhihuishi</i>), 104 lieutenants (<i>qianhu</i>), and 103 sub-lieutenants (<i>bohu</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[p]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-127_224-3"><a href="#cite_note-d7-127-224">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-31_225-2"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-31-225">[210]</a></sup> There were 180 medical personnel, a bureau director<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[q]</a></sup> from the Ministry of Finance, two secretaries, two protocol officers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[r]</a></sup> from the Court of State Ceremonial, an astrological officer, and four astrologers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-31_225-3"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-31-225">[210]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-128_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-d7-128-229">[211]</a></sup> The personnel also had guard judges (<i>wei zhenfu</i>) and battalion judges (<i>suo zhenfu</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-127_224-4"><a href="#cite_note-d7-127-224">[209]</a></sup> The remaining personnel included petty officers (<i>qixiao</i> or <i>quanxiao</i>), brave corps (<i>yongshi</i>), power corps<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[s]</a></sup> (<i>lishi</i>), military soldiers (referred as <i>guanjun</i>, "official soldiers", or <i>qijun</i>, "flag soldiers"), supernumeraries (<i>yuding</i>), boatsman (<i>minshao</i>), buyers (<i>maiban</i>), and clerks (<i>shushou</i>).<b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-128_229-1"><a href="#cite_note-d7-128-229">[211]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-32_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-32-231">[212]</a></sup></b>\n</p><p>Zhu Yunming\'s <i>Xia Xiyang</i> records the following personnel: officers and petty officers (<i>guanxiao</i>), soldiers (<i>qijun</i>), mess leaders (<i>huozhang</i>), helmsman (<i>tuogong</i>), anchormen (<i>bandingshou</i>), interpreters (<i>tongshi</i>), business managers (<i>banshi</i>), accountants (<i>susuanshi</i>), doctors (<i>yishi</i>), anchor mechanics (<i>tiemiao</i>), caulkers (<i>munian</i>), sailmakers (<i>dacai</i>), sailors (<i>shuishou</i>), and boatmen (<i>minshaoren).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-128_229-2"><a href="#cite_note-d7-128-229">[211]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-32_231-1"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-32-231">[212]</a></sup></i>\n</p><p>The Liujiagang inscriptions records <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">Zheng He</a> (&#37165;&#21644;) and <a href="/wiki/Wang_Jinghong" title="Wang Jinghong">Wang Jinghong</a> (&#29579;&#26223;&#24344;) as the principal envoys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-stelelist_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-d7-stelelist-232">[213]</a></sup> It also records Zhu Liang (&#26417;&#33391;), <a href="/wiki/Zhou_Man" title="Zhou Man">Zhou Man</a> (&#21608;&#28415;), <a href="/wiki/Hong_Bao" title="Hong Bao">Hong Bao</a> (&#27946;&#20445;), Yang Zhen (&#26954;&#30495;), and Zhang Da (&#24373;&#36948;) as deputy envoys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-stelelist_232-1"><a href="#cite_note-d7-stelelist-232">[213]</a></sup> The Changle inscription repeats this, but adds Li Xing (&#26446;&#33288;) and Wu Zhong (&#21555;&#24544;) as deputy envoys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-stelelist_232-2"><a href="#cite_note-d7-stelelist-232">[213]</a></sup> All the recorded envoys are noted to have carried the rank of Grand Director in both inscriptions, except Zhang Da who was noted with the rank of Senior Assistant Director in the Liujiagang inscription and the rank of Grand Director in the Changle inscription.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-stelelist_232-3"><a href="#cite_note-d7-stelelist-232">[213]</a></sup> Additionally, the Changle inscription mentions Zhu Zhen (&#26417;&#30495;) and Wang Heng (&#29579;&#34913;) as the brigadiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-391_223-1"><a href="#cite_note-du38-391-223">[208]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-stelelist_232-4"><a href="#cite_note-d7-stelelist-232">[213]</a></sup> These people and unnamed "others" are mentioned on the respective inscriptions as those who have composed it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-stelelist_232-5"><a href="#cite_note-d7-stelelist-232">[213]</a></sup> The Changle inscription also mentions that the Daoist priest Yang Yichu (&#26954;&#19968;&#21021;) begged to erect the respective stele.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-stelelist_232-6"><a href="#cite_note-d7-stelelist-232">[213]</a></sup> The two inscriptions state that Admiral Zheng He and his associates had commanded several tens of thousands of government soldiers and over one hundred oceangoing ships for each voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-126_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-d7-126-233">[214]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-8-9_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-8-9-234">[215]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cakra_Donya.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1248" data-file-width="1664" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Cakra_Donya.JPG/220px-Cakra_Donya.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Cakra_Donya.JPG/330px-Cakra_Donya.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Cakra_Donya.JPG/440px-Cakra_Donya.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cakra_Donya.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Cakra Donya bell was a gift from <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">Zheng He</a> to <a href="/wiki/Samudera_Pasai_Sultanate" title="Samudera Pasai Sultanate">Semudera</a> on <a href="/wiki/Sumatra" title="Sumatra">Sumatra</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>For the first voyage, the fleet had a personnel of 27,800<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-51_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-51-22">[22]</a></sup> or 27,870 men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-10_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-10-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-233_24-18"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-233-24">[24]</a></sup> The treasure fleet comprised a total of 317 ships for this voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev96-87_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-lev96-87-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-10_17-4"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-10-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-233_24-19"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-233-24">[24]</a></sup> It included 62 treasure ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-10_17-5"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-10-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-51_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-51-22">[22]</a></sup> It may also be 63 treasure ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-123_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-d7-123-32">[32]</a></sup> The <i>Mingshi</i> states that the fleet had 62 treasure ships and a crew of 27,800 for the first voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-123_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-d7-123-32">[32]</a></sup> Tan Qian\'s (&#35527;&#36983;) <i>Guoque</i> (&#22283;&#30906;) records 63 treasure ships and a crew of 27,870 for the first voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-123_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-d7-123-32">[32]</a></sup> The <i>Zuiweilu</i> records a personnel of 37,000, but this is probably an error.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-123_32-4"><a href="#cite_note-d7-123-32">[32]</a></sup> Yan Congjian\'s (&#22196;&#24478;&#31777;) <i>Shuyu Shilu</i> records an imperial order for the construction of 250 ships specifically for the voyages to the Western Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-123_32-5"><a href="#cite_note-d7-123-32">[32]</a></sup> This actually refers to two separate imperial orders&#8212;as recorded in the <i>Taizong Shilu</i>&#8212;both to the Nanjing\'s capital guards for 200 ships (&#28023;&#36939;&#33337; <i>haiyunchuan</i>; lit. "seagoing transport ships") on 4 September 1403 and for 50 ships (&#28023;&#33337; <i>haichuan</i>; lit. "seagoing ships") on 1 March 1404.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-117-123_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-117-123-235">[216]</a></sup> However, the <i>Taizong Shilu</i> did not record the purpose for which these 250 ships were constructed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-124_236-0"><a href="#cite_note-d7-124-236">[217]</a></sup> It also records a 2 March 1404 imperial order for Fujian to construct five ships (<i>haichuan</i>) to be used in the voyages to the Western Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-123_32-6"><a href="#cite_note-d7-123-32">[32]</a></sup> These 255 ships plus the 62 treasure ships adds up to the total of 317 ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[t]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-123_32-7"><a href="#cite_note-d7-123-32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p><p>For the second voyage, it is thought that the treasure fleet comprised 249 ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-11_41-5"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-11-41">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-62_208-2"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-62-208">[193]</a></sup> On 5 October 1407, as the <i>Taizong Shilu</i> records, Wang Hao was ordered to supervise the conversion of 249 ships in preparation for embassies to the countries at the Western Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[218]</a></sup> This was close to the date when the second voyage was ordered, thus the fleet likely comprised these 249 ships for the second voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-124_236-1"><a href="#cite_note-d7-124-236">[217]</a></sup> The number of <a href="/wiki/Chinese_treasure_ship" title="Chinese treasure ship">treasure ships</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-63_43-4"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-63-43">[42]</a></sup> or personnel<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-11_41-6"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-11-41">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-63_43-5"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-63-43">[42]</a></sup> is not known.\n</p><p>For the third voyage, Fei Xin\'s <i>Xingcha Shenglan</i> recorded that the fleet had 48 <i>haibo</i> (&#28023;&#33334;; lit. "ocean traders") and a crew of over 27,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-125_239-0"><a href="#cite_note-d7-125-239">[219]</a></sup> Dreyer (2007) states that Fei Xin was probably referring to the treasure ships with <i>haibo</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-67_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-67-240">[220]</a></sup> Yan Congjian\'s <i>Shuyu Zhouzilu</i> and Lu Rong\'s <i>Shuyuan Zaji</i> used the term "treasure ship" instead when they mentioned the 48 ships for this voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-125_239-1"><a href="#cite_note-d7-125-239">[219]</a></sup> Coincidently, the <i>Taizong Shilu</i> recorded the 14 February 1408 imperial order for the construction of 48 treasure ships to the Ministry of Works at Nanjing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-125_239-2"><a href="#cite_note-d7-125-239">[219]</a></sup> These were possibly the 48 treasure ships for the third voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-125_239-3"><a href="#cite_note-d7-125-239">[219]</a></sup> Dreyer (2007) states that the treasure fleet likely had an undisclosed array of support ships besides the 48 treasure ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-125_239-4"><a href="#cite_note-d7-125-239">[219]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ma Huan\'s <i>Yingya Shenglan</i> recorded 63 treasure ships for the fourth voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-126_233-1"><a href="#cite_note-d7-126-233">[214]</a></sup> These were probably accompanied by support ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-126_233-2"><a href="#cite_note-d7-126-233">[214]</a></sup> The fleet was crewed by 28,560 men<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-12-3_70-8"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-12-3-70">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-76_73-7"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-76-73">[69]</a></sup> or 27,670 men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ch98-235_76-16"><a href="#cite_note-ch98-235-76">[72]</a></sup> Fei Xin recorded a personnel of 27,670 for this voyage, but another source recorded 28,560.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-126_233-3"><a href="#cite_note-d7-126-233">[214]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There\'s no record for the number of ships or personnel for the fifth voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-13_84-7"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-13-84">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-126_233-4"><a href="#cite_note-d7-126-233">[214]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 2 October 1419, an order was issued for the construction of 41 treasure ships from an undisclosed shipbuilder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[221]</a></sup> It is possible that Admiral Zheng He made use of these ships for the sixth voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-93_97-8"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-93-97">[92]</a></sup> Most scholars have concluded that these were likely used for the sixth voyage,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-126_233-5"><a href="#cite_note-d7-126-233">[214]</a></sup> but many other treasure ships had already been constructed or were in construction by that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[222]</a></sup> There\'s no specific figure for the ships or personnel of the sixth voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-126_233-6"><a href="#cite_note-d7-126-233">[214]</a></sup> The treasure fleet probably made use of several dozen of the treasure ships which was accompanied by half a dozen support vessels each.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-93_97-9"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-93-97">[92]</a></sup>\n</p><p>For the seventh voyage, the Liujiagang and Changle inscriptions speak of over a hundred large ships (&#24040;&#33334; <i>jubo</i>; lit. "great trading vessels").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-126_233-7"><a href="#cite_note-d7-126-233">[214]</a></sup> This probably included most of the remaining treasure ships according to Dreyer (2007).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-126_233-8"><a href="#cite_note-d7-126-233">[214]</a></sup> The treasure ships were likely accompanied by support ships.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-126_233-9"><a href="#cite_note-d7-126-233">[214]</a></sup> The treasure fleet had 27,550 men as personnel for the voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-126_233-10"><a href="#cite_note-d7-126-233">[214]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_affairs">Military affairs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ming_treasure_voyages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Military affairs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Before the voyages, there was turmoil around the seas near the Chinese coast and distant maritime regions, characterized by piracy, banditry, slave trade, or other illicit activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev96-889_243-0"><a href="#cite_note-lev96-889-243">[223]</a></sup> The treasure fleet had a large number of warships to protect their precious cargo and to secure the maritime routes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lev96-889_243-1"><a href="#cite_note-lev96-889-243">[223]</a></sup> They established a substantial Chinese military presence around the South China Sea and trading cities in southern India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-64_45-7"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-64-45">[44]</a></sup> Especially the early stages of the voyages were characterized by highly militaristic objectives, as the Chinese stabilized the sea passages from hostile entities as well strengthened their own position and maintained their status in the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[224]</a></sup> Even though Admiral Zheng He sailed through the oceans with a military force larger and stronger than any local power, there is no written evidence in historical sources that there was any attempt that they forcibly tried to control the maritime trade&#8212;rather than through exploration and promotion of trade&#8212;in the regions of the South China Sea and Indian Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-65_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-65-56">[53]</a></sup> Nevertheless, Dreyer (2007) adds that it must have been a "terrifying apparition" when the large Chinese fleet came within visible reach before the coastline of a foreign country, bringing any state into submission by the sole sight of it alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[225]</a></sup> From the fourth voyage onwards, the treasure fleet ventured further than their usual end-destination of Calicut to lands beyond, where there would be less direct hostilities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dre7-73_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-dre7-73-246">[226]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The fleet engaged and defeated Chen Zuyi\'s pirate fleet in Palembang, Alakeshvara\'s forces in Ceylon, and Sekandar\'s forces in Semudera, bringing security and stability of the maritime routes via Chinese control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[227]</a></sup> These battles served as a reminder of the tremendous power of Ming China to the countries along the maritime routes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-79_192-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-79-192">[177]</a></sup> Chen Zuyi (of Palembang), Alakeshvara (of Ceylon), and Sekandar (of Semudera) were viewed as hostile threats in their regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-79_192-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-79-192">[177]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On the Malabar coast, Calicut and Cochin were in an intense rivalry, so the Ming decided to intervene by granting special status to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cochin" title="Cochin">Cochin</a> and its ruler <i>Keyili</i> (&#21487;&#20134;&#37324;).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sen16-616-7_248-0"><a href="#cite_note-sen16-616-7-248">[228]</a></sup> For the fifth voyage, Zheng He was instructed to confer a seal upon <i>Keyili</i> of Cochin and enfeoff a mountain in his kingdom as the <i>Zhenguo Zhi Shan</i> (&#37806;&#22283;&#20043;&#23665;, Mountain Which Protects the Country).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sen16-616-7_248-1"><a href="#cite_note-sen16-616-7-248">[228]</a></sup> Zheng He delivered a stone tablet, inscribed with a proclamation composed by the Yongle Emperor, to Cochin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sen16-616-7_248-2"><a href="#cite_note-sen16-616-7-248">[228]</a></sup> As long as Cochin remained under the protection of Ming China, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zamorin" title="Zamorin">Zamorin</a> of Calicut was unable to invade Cochin and a military conflict was averted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sen16-616-7_248-3"><a href="#cite_note-sen16-616-7-248">[228]</a></sup> The cessation of the Ming treasure voyages consequently had negative results for Cochin, as the Zamorin of Calicut would eventually launch an invasion against Cochin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sen16-616-7_248-4"><a href="#cite_note-sen16-616-7-248">[228]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In Malacca, the Chinese actively sought to develop a commercial hub and a base of operation for the voyages into the Indian Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-se16-615_184-1"><a href="#cite_note-se16-615-184">[170]</a></sup> Malacca had been a relatively insignificant region, not even qualifying as a polity prior to the voyages according to both Ma Huan and Fei Xin, and was a vassal region of Siam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-se16-615_184-2"><a href="#cite_note-se16-615-184">[170]</a></sup> In 1405, the Ming court dispatched Zheng He with a stone tablet enfeoffing the Western Mountain of Malacca as well as an imperial order elevating the status of the port to a country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-se16-615_184-3"><a href="#cite_note-se16-615-184">[170]</a></sup> The Chinese also established a government depot (&#23448;&#24288;) as a fortified cantonment for their soldiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-se16-615_184-4"><a href="#cite_note-se16-615-184">[170]</a></sup> Ma Huan reported that Siam did not dare to invade Malacca thereafter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-se16-615_184-5"><a href="#cite_note-se16-615-184">[170]</a></sup> The rulers of Malacca, such as King Paramesvara (&#30333;&#37324;&#36855;&#34311;&#21068;) in 1411, would pay tribute to the Chinese emperor in person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-se16-615_184-6"><a href="#cite_note-se16-615-184">[170]</a></sup> In 1431, when a Malaccan representative complained that Siam was obstructing tribute missions to the Ming court, the Xuande Emperor dispatched Zheng He carrying a threatening message for the Siamese king saying "You, king should respect my orders, develop good relations with your neighbours, examine and instruct your subordinates and not act recklessly or aggressively."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-se16-615_184-7"><a href="#cite_note-se16-615-184">[170]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diplomacy_and_commerce">Diplomacy and commerce</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ming_treasure_voyages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Diplomacy and commerce">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Diplomacy_and_commerce_during_the_Ming_treasure_voyages"
