title="Diplomacy and commerce during the Ming treasure voyages">Diplomacy and commerce during the Ming treasure voyages</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Room_95-6753.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2460" data-file-width="2460" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Room_95-6753.JPG/220px-Room_95-6753.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Room_95-6753.JPG/330px-Room_95-6753.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Room_95-6753.JPG/440px-Room_95-6753.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Room_95-6753.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Porcelain" title="Porcelain">Porcelain</a> wares, such as those similar to these Yongle-era porcelain flasks from Jingdezhen, were often presented as trade goods during the expeditions. (British Museum)</div></div></div>\n<p>The treasure ships had an enormous cargo of various products.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fin8-337_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-fin8-337-249">[229]</a></sup> Admiral Zheng returned to China with many kinds of tribute goods, such as silver, spices, sandalwood, precious stones, ivory, ebony, camphor, tin, deer hides, coral, kingfisher feathers, tortoise shells, gums and resin, rhinoceros horn, sapanwood and safflower (for dyes and drugs), Indian cotton cloth, and ambergris (for perfume).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fin8-337_249-1"><a href="#cite_note-fin8-337-249">[229]</a></sup> They even brought back exotic animals, such as ostriches, elephants, and giraffes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fin8-337_249-2"><a href="#cite_note-fin8-337-249">[229]</a></sup> The imports from the voyages provided large quantities of economic goods that fueled China\'s own industries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ra87-158_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-ra87-158-250">[230]</a></sup> For instance, there was so much <a href="/wiki/Cobalt_blue" title="Cobalt blue">cobalt oxide</a> from Persia that the porcelain industry at <a href="/wiki/Jingdezhen" title="Jingdezhen">Jingdezhen</a> had a plentiful supply for decades after the voyages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fin8-337_249-3"><a href="#cite_note-fin8-337-249">[229]</a></sup> The fleet also returned with such a large amount of black pepper that the once-costly luxury became a common commodity in Chinese society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fin8-337_249-4"><a href="#cite_note-fin8-337-249">[229]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[231]</a></sup> There were sometimes so many Chinese goods unloaded into an Indian port that it could take months to price everything.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ra87-158_250-1"><a href="#cite_note-ra87-158-250">[230]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-br98-616_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-br98-616-13">[13]</a></sup> The treasure voyages resulted in a flourishing Ming economy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[232]</a></sup> while boosting the lucrative maritime commerce significantly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[233]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[234]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The commodities that the ships carried included three major categories: gifts to be bestowed on rulers, items for exchange of goods or payment of goods with fixed prices at low rates (e.g. gold, silver, copper coins, and paper money), and items in which China had the monopoly (e.g. musks, ceramics, and silks).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r87a-com_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-r87a-com-255">[235]</a></sup> However, the Ming trade enterprise also saw significant changes and developments in which the Chinese themselves began trading and supplying the commodities that were non-Chinese in origin and earlier entirely in the hands of the Indians, Arabs, and other foreigners.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r87a-com_255-1"><a href="#cite_note-r87a-com-255">[235]</a></sup> This highlighted the commercial character of the voyages, in which the Chinese further expanded upon the already large profits from their trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-r87a-com_255-2"><a href="#cite_note-r87a-com-255">[235]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The impact of the Ming expeditions on commerce was on multiple levels: it established imperial control over local private commercial networks, expanded tributary relations and thereby brought commerce under state supervision, established court-supervised transactions at foreign ports and thereby generate substantial revenue for both parties, and increased production and circulation of commodities across the region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-se16-624-6_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-se16-624-6-256">[236]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Imperial proclamations were issued to foreign kings, meaning that they could either submit and be bestowed with rewards or refuse and be pacified under the threat of an overwhelming military force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-33_150-1"><a href="#cite_note-d7-33-150">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mil70-1-2_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-mil70-1-2-257">[237]</a></sup> Foreign kings had to reaffirm their recognition of the Chinese emperor\'s superior status by presenting tribute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[238]</a></sup> Many countries were enrolled as tributaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fair49-140_162-3"><a href="#cite_note-fair49-140-162">[148]</a></sup> The treasure fleet conducted the transport of the many foreign envoys to China and back, but some envoys traveled independently.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[239]</a></sup> Those rulers who submitted received political protection and material rewards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mil70-2_220-1"><a href="#cite_note-mil70-2-220">[205]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[240]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography_and_society">Geography and society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ming_treasure_voyages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Geography and society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tribute_Giraffe_with_Attendant.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="759" decoding="async" height="435" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Tribute_Giraffe_with_Attendant.jpg/220px-Tribute_Giraffe_with_Attendant.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Tribute_Giraffe_with_Attendant.jpg/330px-Tribute_Giraffe_with_Attendant.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Tribute_Giraffe_with_Attendant.jpg/440px-Tribute_Giraffe_with_Attendant.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tribute_Giraffe_with_Attendant.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Yongle Emperor commissioned Shen Du to make this painting of the tribute giraffe, presented by Bengal on 20 September 1414 (Philadelphia Museum of Art).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[241]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>During the onset of their voyages, the treasure fleet would embark from the Longjiang shipyard, north-west of Nanjing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-9_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-9-262">[242]</a></sup> They would then sail down the Yangtze River to Liujiagang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-9_262-1"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-9-262">[242]</a></sup> Once there, Admiral Zheng He would organize his fleet and make sacrifices to Tianfei.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-9_262-2"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-9-262">[242]</a></sup> Over the course of the following four to eight weeks, the fleet would gradually proceed to Taiping anchorage in Changle, Fujian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-9_262-3"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-9-262">[242]</a></sup> There, the fleet would wait for the favorable <a href="/wiki/Monsoon#Northeast_monsoon" title="Monsoon">northeast monsoon</a> of winter<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-263"><a href="#cite_note-263">[u]</a></sup> before leaving the Fujian coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-358_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-du38-358-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-9_262-4"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-9-262">[242]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-30_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-30-264">[243]</a></sup> They would reach the sea through the Wuhumen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-358_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-du38-358-26">[26]</a></sup> For the voyages, the fleet always visited the port Qui Nhon (in Champa) first.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-52_25-6"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-52-25">[25]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the first three voyages from 1405 to 1411, the fleet followed the same basic maritime route: from Fujian to the first call in <a href="/wiki/Champa" title="Champa">Champa</a>, across the <a href="/wiki/South_China_Sea" title="South China Sea">South China Sea</a> to <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sumatra" title="Sumatra">Sumatra</a>, up the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Malacca" title="Strait of Malacca">Strait of Malacca</a> to northern <a href="/wiki/Sumatra" title="Sumatra">Sumatra</a> for assembly of the fleet, across the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Ocean" title="Indian Ocean">Indian Ocean</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ceylon" title="Ceylon">Ceylon</a>, then along the <a href="/wiki/Malabar_Coast" title="Malabar Coast">Malabar Coast</a> to <a href="/wiki/Kozhikode" title="Kozhikode">Calicut</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[244]</a></sup> For the time being, the fleet sailed up to and no further than Calicut.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dre7-73_246-1"><a href="#cite_note-dre7-73-246">[226]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chu8-2355_219-1"><a href="#cite_note-chu8-2355-219">[204]</a></sup> During the fourth voyage, the route was extended to Hormuz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-30-2_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-30-2-266">[245]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chu8-2355_219-2"><a href="#cite_note-chu8-2355-219">[204]</a></sup> During the fifth, sixth, and seventh voyage, the fleet proceeded further to other destinations at the Arabian Peninsula and East Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-30-2_266-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-30-2-266">[245]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chu8-2355_219-3"><a href="#cite_note-chu8-2355-219">[204]</a></sup> For the sixth voyage, the treasure fleet sailed up to Calicut, where several detached squadrons proceeded to further destinations at the Arabian Peninsula and East Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-30-2_266-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-30-2-266">[245]</a></sup> For the seventh voyage, the treasure fleet followed the route up to Hormuz, while detached squadrons traveled to the other far-lying destinations at the Arabian Peninsula and East Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-30-2_266-3"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-30-2-266">[245]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The treasure fleet sailed the equatorial and subtropical waters of the South China Sea and Indian Ocean, where they were dependent on the circumstances of the annual cycle of monsoon winds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-27_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-27-267">[246]</a></sup> The return journey was set during the late summer and early autumn, because favorable monsoon winds would be present during this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[247]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During all the voyages, the fleet departed from Sumatra to sail westward across the Indian Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-69_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-69-269">[248]</a></sup> The journey from northern Sumatra to Ceylon involved sailing for about two to four weeks without laying sight on land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-30_264-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-30-264">[243]</a></sup> The first part of Ceylon that would be visible after departing from Sumatra was Namanakuli (or Parrot\'s Beak Mountain), the eastern-most mountain (6680&#160;ft in elevation and 45 miles away from the coast).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-69_269-1"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-69-269">[248]</a></sup> Two or three days after sighting this geographical feature, the treasure fleet would adjust their course to sail south of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dondra_Head" title="Dondra Head">Dondra Head</a> at Ceylon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-69_269-2"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-69-269">[248]</a></sup> The fleet would have been at sea for a considerable long time by then since departing from Sumatra, thus they would make a call at a port in Ceylon, usually at Beruwala and sometimes at Galle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[249]</a></sup> Although they have made port at both locations, it was clear that the fleet\'s preference was Beruwala over Galle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dre7-71_53-2"><a href="#cite_note-dre7-71-53">[50]</a></sup> Ma Huan characterized Beruwala as "the wharf of the country of Ceylon."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dre7-71_53-3"><a href="#cite_note-dre7-71-53">[50]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Northern Sumatra was an important region for the fleet\'s anchorage and assembly before they proceeded through the Indian Ocean to Ceylon and southern India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-77_75-12"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-77-75">[71]</a></sup> Mainly its location rather than its wealth or products were important to the fleet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-153_133-7"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-153-133">[124]</a></sup> Ma Huan stated that Semudera was the main route to the Western Ocean,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[250]</a></sup> characterizing it as the most important port of assembly for the Western Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-77_75-13"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-77-75">[71]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Ming China had cordial relations with Calicut, which was valuable as they tried to extend the tributary system to the states around the Indian Ocean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey07-61_190-2"><a href="#cite_note-drey07-61-190">[175]</a></sup> Ma Huan described Calicut as the "great country of the Western Ocean".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-54_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-54-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-77_75-14"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-77-75">[71]</a></sup> He was very positive about the Calicut authorities\' regulation of trade and attention to weights and measurements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-54_31-3"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-54-31">[31]</a></sup> Fei Xin described Calicut as the "great harbor" of the Western Ocean countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-155_135-12"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-155-135">[126]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Fei_Xin" title="Fei Xin">Fei Xin</a> wrote that the people of Mogadishu were bigoted and insincere (<i>wangyin</i>, both words can also mean "stupid").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-88_272-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-88-272">[251]</a></sup> It was further mentioned that they often drilled their soldiers and practiced archery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-88_272-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-88-272">[251]</a></sup> In contrast, Fei Xin characterized the people of Brava as pure and honest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-88_272-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-88-272">[251]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Navigation">Navigation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ming_treasure_voyages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Navigation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:616px;max-width:616px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mao_Kun_Map_-_stellar_diagram_1_-_P1100089.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Mao_Kun_Map_-_stellar_diagram_1_-_P1100089.JPG/150px-Mao_Kun_Map_-_stellar_diagram_1_-_P1100089.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Mao_Kun_Map_-_stellar_diagram_1_-_P1100089.JPG/225px-Mao_Kun_Map_-_stellar_diagram_1_-_P1100089.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Mao_Kun_Map_-_stellar_diagram_1_-_P1100089.JPG/300px-Mao_Kun_Map_-_stellar_diagram_1_-_P1100089.JPG 2x" width="150"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Deogarh to Hormuz</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mao_Kun_Map_-_stellar_diagram_2_-_P1100090.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Mao_Kun_Map_-_stellar_diagram_2_-_P1100090.JPG/150px-Mao_Kun_Map_-_stellar_diagram_2_-_P1100090.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Mao_Kun_Map_-_stellar_diagram_2_-_P1100090.JPG/225px-Mao_Kun_Map_-_stellar_diagram_2_-_P1100090.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Mao_Kun_Map_-_stellar_diagram_2_-_P1100090.JPG/300px-Mao_Kun_Map_-_stellar_diagram_2_-_P1100090.JPG 2x" width="150"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Ceylon to Semudera</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mao_Kun_Map_-_stellar_diagram_3_-_P1100092.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Mao_Kun_Map_-_stellar_diagram_3_-_P1100092.JPG/150px-Mao_Kun_Map_-_stellar_diagram_3_-_P1100092.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Mao_Kun_Map_-_stellar_diagram_3_-_P1100092.JPG/225px-Mao_Kun_Map_-_stellar_diagram_3_-_P1100092.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Mao_Kun_Map_-_stellar_diagram_3_-_P1100092.JPG/300px-Mao_Kun_Map_-_stellar_diagram_3_-_P1100092.JPG 2x" width="150"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Poulo Rondo to Ceylon</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mao_Kun_Map_-_stellar_diagram_4_-_P1100093.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Mao_Kun_Map_-_stellar_diagram_4_-_P1100093.JPG/150px-Mao_Kun_Map_-_stellar_diagram_4_-_P1100093.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Mao_Kun_Map_-_stellar_diagram_4_-_P1100093.JPG/225px-Mao_Kun_Map_-_stellar_diagram_4_-_P1100093.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Mao_Kun_Map_-_stellar_diagram_4_-_P1100093.JPG/300px-Mao_Kun_Map_-_stellar_diagram_4_-_P1100093.JPG 2x" width="150"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Hormuz to Calicut</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">The <i><a href="/wiki/Wubei_Zhi" title="Wubei Zhi">Wubei Zhi</a></i> includes four stellar diagrams appended to the Mao Kun map. These charts were derived from records of Zheng He\'s navigators.</div></div></div>\n<p>Admiral Zheng He followed for the most part established trade routes during his voyages rather than unknown territory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fair49-141_169-1"><a href="#cite_note-fair49-141-169">[155]</a></sup> During the voyages, the crew acquired and collected a large amount of navigational data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-br98-616-7_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-br98-616-7-273">[252]</a></sup> The astrological officer and his four astrologers specifically recorded the astronomical data.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-239-0_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-239-0-274">[253]</a></sup> The general mass of navigational data were processed into different kinds of charts by a cartographic office.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-br98-616-7_273-1"><a href="#cite_note-br98-616-7-273">[252]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-239-0_274-1"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-239-0-274">[253]</a></sup> The office included an astrological officer, four astrologers, and their clerks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-239-0_274-2"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-239-0-274">[253]</a></sup> This provided the expeditionary commanders with the necessary navigational charts for their voyages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-br98-616-7_273-2"><a href="#cite_note-br98-616-7-273">[252]</a></sup> Many copies of the expeditionary charts were housed in the Ministry of War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-br98-616-7_273-3"><a href="#cite_note-br98-616-7-273">[252]</a></sup> Additional navigational data were probably also supplied by local <a href="/wiki/Maritime_pilot" title="Maritime pilot">maritime pilots</a>, Arab records, Indian records, and earlier Chinese records.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-239-0_274-3"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-239-0-274">[253]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Mao_Kun_map" title="Mao Kun map">Mao Kun map</a> is associated with the route of the voyages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[254]</a></sup> The map is collected in the <i><a href="/wiki/Wubei_Zhi" title="Wubei Zhi">Wubei Zhi</a></i>, compiled by Mao Yuanyi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chu8-2355-6_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-chu8-2355-6-276">[255]</a></sup> It depicts various geographic locations, from Nanjing to Hormuz as well as the East African coast, with routes illustrated by dotted lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chu8-2355-6_276-1"><a href="#cite_note-chu8-2355-6-276">[255]</a></sup> The directions are expressed by <a href="/wiki/Compass" title="Compass">compass</a> points and distances, with references to navigational techniques (such as <a href="/wiki/Depth_sounding" title="Depth sounding">depth sounding</a> to avoid shallow waters) and astronomy (particularly along the north&#8211;south route of Africa where the latitude is determined by the height of constellations relative to the horizon).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chu8-2355-6_276-2"><a href="#cite_note-chu8-2355-6-276">[255]</a></sup> The distances are expressed by time according to a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Watch_system" title="Watch system">watch system</a> (two hour periods).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chu8-2355-6_276-3"><a href="#cite_note-chu8-2355-6-276">[255]</a></sup> The Mao Kun map is appended by four stellar diagrams used for determining the position of the ship in relation to the stars and constellations on specific sections of the maritime route.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chu8-2355-6_276-4"><a href="#cite_note-chu8-2355-6-276">[255]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Faith_and_ceremony">Faith and ceremony</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ming_treasure_voyages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Faith and ceremony">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:350px; border-width: 1px; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303512" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>\n<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">The power of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tianfei" title="Tianfei">goddess</a>, having indeed been manifested in previous times, has been abundantly revealed in the present generation. In the midst of the rushing waters it happened that, when there was a hurricane, suddenly a <a href="/wiki/St._Elmo%27s_fire" title="St. Elmo\'s fire">divine lantern</a> was seen shining at the masthead, and as soon as that miraculous light appeared the danger was appeased, so that even in the peril of capsizing one felt reassured and that there was no cause for fear.</div>\n<p><cite class="right-aligned" style="">&#8212; <i>Admiral Zheng He and his associates about witnessing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tianfei" title="Tianfei">Tianfei</a>\'s divine lantern, which represented the natural phenomena <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Elmo%27s_fire" title="Saint Elmo\'s fire">Saint Elmo\'s fire</a></i> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nee59-558_136-1"><a href="#cite_note-nee59-558-136">[127]</a></sup></cite>\n</p>\n</div>\n<p>The faith of the treasure fleet\'s crew centered around <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mazu_(goddess)" title="Mazu (goddess)">Tianfei</a>, the "Heavenly Princess" who was the goddess of sailors and seafarers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-148_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-148-277">[256]</a></sup> The Liujiagang and Changle inscriptions suggest that Zheng He\'s life was mostly defined by the treasure voyages and that his devotion to Tianfei was the dominant faith that he adhered to.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-150_278-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-150-278">[257]</a></sup> The two inscriptions honored and commemorated the goddess <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tianfei" title="Tianfei">Tianfei</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[258]</a></sup> Admiral Zheng He and his associates had established these inscriptions at the temples of Tianfei at Liujiagang on 14 March 1431 and Changle between 5 December 1431 and 3 January 1432.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[259]</a></sup> These inscriptions make reference to the crew witnessing <a href="/wiki/St._Elmo%27s_fire" title="St. Elmo\'s fire">St. Elmo\'s fire</a> during dangerous storms and interpreting it as a sign of divine protection by Tianfei.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-281"><a href="#cite_note-281">[260]</a></sup> The Liujiagang and Changle inscriptions are considered the epitaphs of the treasure voyages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-135_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-135-125">[117]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In Galle at Ceylon, Admiral Zheng He set up a <a href="/wiki/Galle_Trilingual_Inscription" title="Galle Trilingual Inscription">trilingual inscription</a> dated 15 February 1409.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[v]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-drey7-66_52-6"><a href="#cite_note-drey7-66-52">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ne71-galle_283-0"><a href="#cite_note-ne71-galle-283">[261]</a></sup> The inscription is in three languages, in which the Chinese section praised the Buddha, the Tamil section praised the local god Tenavarai Nayanar who was an incarnation of <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a>, and the Persian section praised <a href="/wiki/Allah" title="Allah">Allah</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ne71-galle_283-1"><a href="#cite_note-ne71-galle-283">[261]</a></sup> Each section contains a similar lists of offerings, including items such as 1000 pieces of gold, 5000 pieces of silver, 100 rolls of silk, 2500 catties of perfumed oil, and a variety of bronze ornaments.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dre7-71_53-4"><a href="#cite_note-dre7-71-53">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-284"><a href="#cite_note-284">[262]</a></sup> Thus, as shown by this inscription, the Chinese paid their respect to the three religions that were dominant in Ceylon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-148_277-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-148-277">[256]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ne71-galle_283-2"><a href="#cite_note-ne71-galle-283">[261]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 20 September 1414, Bengali envoys presented a tribute giraffe in the name of King <a href="/wiki/Saifuddin_Hamza_Shah" title="Saifuddin Hamza Shah">Saif Al-Din Hamzah Shah</a> of Bengal (r. 1410&#8211;1412) to the Yongle Emperor of Ming China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[263]</a></sup> The giraffe was presented as the <a href="/wiki/Qilin" title="Qilin">qilin</a>, but this association was met with a dismissive attitude from the Yongle Emperor who rejected the laudatory <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Official_Communications_of_the_Chinese_Empire#Memorials" title="Official Communications of the Chinese Empire">memorials</a> of his officials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-286"><a href="#cite_note-286">[264]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Records_and_literature">Records and literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ming_treasure_voyages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Records and literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:YingYaiShengLan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="652" data-file-width="790" decoding="async" height="182" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/YingYaiShengLan.jpg/220px-YingYaiShengLan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/YingYaiShengLan.jpg/330px-YingYaiShengLan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/YingYaiShengLan.jpg/440px-YingYaiShengLan.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:YingYaiShengLan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pages from a copy of the <i>Yingya Shenglan</i></div></div></div>\n<p>There are several extant contemporary accounts, including <a href="/wiki/Ma_Huan" title="Ma Huan">Ma Huan</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Yingya_Shenglan" title="Yingya Shenglan">Yingya Shenglan</a></i> [&#28699;&#28079;&#21213;&#35261;],<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[w]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fei_Xin" title="Fei Xin">Fei Xin</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Xingcha_Shenglan" title="Xingcha Shenglan">Xingcha Shenglan</a></i> [&#26143;&#27086;&#21213;&#35261;], and <a href="/wiki/Gong_Zhen" title="Gong Zhen">Gong Zhen</a>\'s <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xiyang_Fanguo_Zhi" title="Xiyang Fanguo Zhi">Xiyang Fanguo Zhi</a></i> [&#35199;&#27915;&#30058;&#22283;&#24535;].<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-288"><a href="#cite_note-288">[265]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[266]</a></sup> Ma Huan served as an interpreter on the fourth, sixth, and seventh voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-35_146-2"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-35-146">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-6-7_290-0"><a href="#cite_note-d7-6-7-290">[267]</a></sup> Guo Chongli was Ma Huan\'s collaborator on the <i>Yingya Shenglan</i> and participated in three of the expeditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-55_291-0"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-55-291">[268]</a></sup> Fei Xin served as a soldier on the third, fifth, and seventh expedition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-6-7_290-1"><a href="#cite_note-d7-6-7-290">[267]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[269]</a></sup> Gong Zhen served as Zheng He\'s private secretary on the seventh voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-6-7_290-2"><a href="#cite_note-d7-6-7-290">[267]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293"><a href="#cite_note-293">[270]</a></sup> These three sources provide insightful observations to the political, economic, social, cultural, and religious conditions of the lands visited throughout the voyages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chu8-2356_294-0"><a href="#cite_note-chu8-2356-294">[271]</a></sup> In addition, the Liujiagang and Changle inscriptions are valuable records by Admiral Zheng He and his associates themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-295"><a href="#cite_note-295">[272]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Ming_Shilu" title="Ming Shilu">Ming Shilu</a></i> also provides a lot of the information relating to the treasure voyages,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-217-8_296-0"><a href="#cite_note-d7-217-8-296">[273]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chu8-2356_294-1"><a href="#cite_note-chu8-2356-294">[271]</a></sup> particularly the exchange of ambassadors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chu8-2356_294-2"><a href="#cite_note-chu8-2356-294">[271]</a></sup> The work is divided into individual sections about the reigns of Ming emperors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-217-8_296-1"><a href="#cite_note-d7-217-8-296">[273]</a></sup> Zheng He lived through the reigns of five Ming emperors,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-217-8_296-2"><a href="#cite_note-d7-217-8-296">[273]</a></sup> but he directly served three emperors in his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-6_297-0"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-6-297">[274]</a></sup> He is mentioned in the <i>Taizong Shilu</i> of the Yongle reign, the <i>Renzong Shilu</i> of the Hongxi reign, and the <i>Xuanzong Shilu</i> of the Xuande reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-217-8_296-3"><a href="#cite_note-d7-217-8-296">[273]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <i>Taizong Shilu</i> had combined the second and third voyages into one expedition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-8-9_234-1"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-8-9-234">[215]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-95_298-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-95-298">[275]</a></sup> This record was followed by the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mingshi" title="Mingshi">Mingshi</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-95_298-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-95-298">[275]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299"><a href="#cite_note-299">[276]</a></sup> This caused Zheng He\'s Palembang journey of 1424&#8211;25<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[x]</a></sup> to be wrongly construed as the sixth voyage to make up for the seven voyages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-du38-387_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-du38-387-114">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-8-9_234-2"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-8-9-234">[215]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-95_298-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-95-298">[275]</a></sup> However, the Liujiagang and Changle inscriptions made a clear distinction between the second and third voyage as they correctly date the second voyage from 1407 to 1409 and the third voyage from 1409 to 1411.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-8-9_234-3"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-8-9-234">[215]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-duy38-361_301-0"><a href="#cite_note-duy38-361-301">[277]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[278]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A number of later works have been preserved. The accounts in the <i>Mingshi</i> (1739) and Huang Xingzeng\'s <i>Xiyang Chaogong Dianlu</i> [&#35199;&#27915;&#26397;&#36002;&#20856;&#37636;] (1520) rely on Ma Huan\'s original <i>Yingya Shenglan</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-54_303-0"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-54-303">[279]</a></sup> Zheng Xiao\'s <i>Wuxuebian</i> [&#21566;&#23416;&#32232;] (ca. 1522) relies on Zhang Sheng\'s "rifacimento" of Ma Huan\'s <i>Yingya Shenglan</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304">[y]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-54_303-1"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-54-303">[279]</a></sup> Zhu Yunming\'s <i>Qianwen Ji</i> [A Record of Things Once Heard] (ca. 1526) contains his <i>Xia Xiyang</i> [&#19979;&#35199;&#27915;; Down the Western Ocean], which provides a detailed itinerary of the seventh voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-150_278-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-150-278">[257]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mi70-xi_305-0"><a href="#cite_note-mi70-xi-305">[280]</a></sup> There are also Lu Rong\'s <i>Shuyuan Zaji</i> [&#33789;&#22290;&#38620;&#35352;; Bean Garden Miscellany] (1475),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-219-0_306-0"><a href="#cite_note-d7-219-0-306">[281]</a></sup> <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Yan_Gongjian&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Yan Gongjian (page does not exist)">Yan Gongjian</a>\'s <i>Shuyu Zhouzilu</i> [&#27530;&#22495;&#21608;&#21672;&#37636;; Record of Despatches Concerning the Different Countries] (1520),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-219-0_306-1"><a href="#cite_note-d7-219-0-306">[281]</a></sup> <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Gu_Qiyuan&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Gu Qiyuan (page does not exist)">Gu Qiyuan</a>\'s <i>Kezuo Zhuiyu</i> [&#23458;&#24231;&#36101;&#35486;; Boring Talks for My Guests] (ca. 1628).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-219-0_306-2"><a href="#cite_note-d7-219-0-306">[281]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mao_Yuanyi" title="Mao Yuanyi">Mao Yuanyi</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Wubei_Zhi" title="Wubei Zhi">Wubei Zhi</a></i> [&#27494;&#20633;&#24535;] (1628) preserved the Mao Kun map [&#33541;&#22372;&#22294;], which is largely based on material from the treasure voyages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-219-0_306-3"><a href="#cite_note-d7-219-0-306">[281]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Luo Maodeng\'s <i>Sanbao Taijian Xia Xiyang Ji Tongsu Yanyi</i> [&#19977;&#23542;&#22826;&#30435;&#19979;&#35199;&#27915;&#35352;&#36890;&#20439;&#28436;&#32681;] (1597) is a fiction novel about the exploits of Admiral Zheng He and his fleet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d7-6-7_290-3"><a href="#cite_note-d7-6-7-290">[267]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finl92-232_307-0"><a href="#cite_note-finl92-232-307">[282]</a></sup> In the preface, Luo states that Chinese maritime power was essential to maintaining the world order.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[283]</a></sup> In Luo\'s work, Admiral Zheng He sailed the oceans in search for a sacred imperial seal to restore harmony in the Middle Kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finl92-232_307-1"><a href="#cite_note-finl92-232-307">[282]</a></sup> He never finds this seal in the story, giving the suggestion that the world order cannot be restored by any other means than military force as noted in Finlay (1992).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-309"><a href="#cite_note-309">[284]</a></sup> Luo Maodeng\'s novel contains a description of different classes of ships with their sizes: the 36 nine-masted treasure ships (<i>baochuan</i>) were 44.4 by 18 zhang, the 700 eight-masted horse ships (<i>machuan</i>) were 37 by 15 zhang, the 240 seven-masted grain ships or supply ships (<i>liangchuan</i>) were 28 by 12 zhang, the 300 six-masted billet ships or troop transports (<i>zuochuan</i>) were 24 by 9.4 zhang, and the 180 five-masted combat ships or warships proper (<i>zhanchuan</i>) were 18 by 6.8 zhang.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-310"><a href="#cite_note-310">[285]</a></sup> Dreyer (2007) argues that this work holds little to no evidential value as a historical source, but also notes that Duyvendak thinks that it may be based on some truth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-311"><a href="#cite_note-311">[286]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <i>Kezuo Zhuiyu</i> and the <i>Shuyu Zhouzilu</i> describes the following circumstances of what happened to the official archives about the expeditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d38-395-6_312-0"><a href="#cite_note-d38-395-6-312">[287]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Chenghua_Emperor" title="Chenghua Emperor">Chenghua Emperor</a> issued an order to retrieve the documents concerning the expeditions to the Western Ocean from the Ministry of War archives, but the official Liu Daxia (&#21129;&#22823;&#22799;) had hidden and burned the documents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d38-395-6_312-1"><a href="#cite_note-d38-395-6-312">[287]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-173-5_313-0"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-173-5-313">[288]</a></sup> Liu Daxia dismissed the accounts as "deceitful exaggerations of bizarre things far removed from the testimony of people\'s ears and eyes."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d38-395-6_312-2"><a href="#cite_note-d38-395-6-312">[287]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-173-5_313-1"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-173-5-313">[288]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chu8-2356_294-3"><a href="#cite_note-chu8-2356-294">[271]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <i>Shuyu Zhouzilu</i> then adds the following to the story.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d38-395-6_312-3"><a href="#cite_note-d38-395-6-312">[287]</a></sup> The Minister of War Xiang Zhong (&#38917;&#24544;; in office 1474&#8211;1477) had sent a clerk to retrieve the documents, but the clerk could not find them after several days of searching.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d38-395-6_312-4"><a href="#cite_note-d38-395-6-312">[287]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-173-5_313-2"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-173-5-313">[288]</a></sup> Liu Daxia eventually confessed and justified his actions to Xiang Zhong by stating that "the expeditions of Sanbao to the Western Ocean wasted tens of myriads of money and grain, and moreover the people who met their deaths [on these expeditions] may be counted by the myriads. Although he returned with wonderful things, what benefit was it to the state? This was merely an action of bad government of which ministers should severely disapprove. Even if the old archives were still preserved they should be destroyed in order to suppress [a repetition of these things] at the root."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d38-395-6_312-5"><a href="#cite_note-d38-395-6-312">[287]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-173-5_313-3"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-173-5-313">[288]</a></sup> Xiang Zhong was recorded to have been impressed by this explanation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-d38-395-6_312-6"><a href="#cite_note-d38-395-6-312">[287]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-173-5_313-4"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-173-5-313">[288]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <i>Mingshi</i>,  the <i>Xuanzong Shilu</i>,  and the <i>Mingshi Jishi Benmo</i> [&#26126;&#21490;&#32000;&#20107;&#26412;&#26411;] attributes the reason for the suppression and destruction of the archived records to preventing that the powerful eunuch Wang Zhi (&#27754;&#30452;) could consult it for his invasion of Vietnam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314"><a href="#cite_note-314">[289]</a></sup> Dreyer (2007) notes that Liu Daxia couldn\'t have had access to the records in his capacity at the time, thus raising doubt about his actual involvement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dr7-173-5_313-5"><a href="#cite_note-dr7-173-5-313">[288]</a></sup> Duyvendak (1939) stated that the Ministry of War officials weren\'t influential enough to stop the retrieval of the documents and therefore speculates that Liu Daxia may have destroyed them with the approval of the Minister of War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315"><a href="#cite_note-315">[290]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ming_treasure_voyages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:350px; border-width: 1px; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303512" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/>\n<div class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">It is now about 80 years since there arrived in this city of Chalicut certain vessels of white Christians, who wore their hair long like Germans, and had no beards except around the mouth, such as are worn at Constantinople by cavaliers and courtiers. They landed, wearing a cuirass, helmet, and visor, and carrying a certain weapon [sword] attached to a spear. Their vessels are armed with <a href="/wiki/Bombard_(weapon)" title="Bombard (weapon)">bombards</a>, shorter than those in use with us. Once every two years they return with 20 or 25 vessels. They are unable to tell what people they are, nor what merchandise they bring to this city, save that it includes very fine linen-cloth and brass-ware. They load spices. Their vessels have four masts like those of Spain. If they were Germans it seems to me that we should have had some notice about them; possibly they may be Russians if they have a port there. On the arrival of the <a href="/wiki/Vasco_da_Gama" title="Vasco da Gama">captain</a> we may learn who these people are, for the <a href="/wiki/Gaspar_da_Gama" title="Gaspar da Gama">Italian-speaking pilot</a>, who was given him by the Moorish king, and whom he took away contrary to his inclinations, is with him, and may be able to tell.</div>\n<p><cite class="right-aligned" style="">&#8212; <i>Girolamo Sernigi (1499) about the then-unknown Chinese visitors</i> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-316"><a href="#cite_note-316">[291]</a></sup></cite>\n</p>\n</div>\n<p>In 1499, shortly before <a href="/wiki/Vasco_da_Gama" title="Vasco da Gama">Vasco da Gama</a>\'s return from India to Portugal, <a href="/wiki/Girolamo_Sernigi" title="Girolamo Sernigi">Girolamo Sernigi</a> reported on the Portuguese accounts from da Gama\'s expedition that "certain vessels of white Christians" had made port at Calicut on the Malabar coast generations before their arrival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finl92-225_317-0"><a href="#cite_note-finl92-225-317">[292]</a></sup> He speculated that these unknown mariners could have been the Germans or the Russians, but concluded that "on the arrival of the captain [da Gama] we may learn who these people are."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finl92-225-gs_318-0"><a href="#cite_note-finl92-225-gs-318">[293]</a></sup> After his arrival at Calicut, Vasco da Gama began hearing tales of pale bearded men who sailed with their giant ships along the local coastal waters of Calicut generations before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wil98-335_319-0"><a href="#cite_note-wil98-335-319">[294]</a></sup> The Portuguese were not aware that these tales were about Zheng He\'s fleets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wil98-335_319-1"><a href="#cite_note-wil98-335-319">[294]</a></sup> They would eventually discover that these unknown mariners were, in fact, the Chinese.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finl92-225_317-1"><a href="#cite_note-finl92-225-317">[292]</a></sup> Da Gama\'s men were apparently even mistaken for the Chinese at first on arrival at the <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East African</a> coast, because the Chinese had been the last-seen pale-skinned strangers arriving with large wooden ships in the memories of the East African people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finl92-225_317-2"><a href="#cite_note-finl92-225-317">[292]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In Calicut, da Gama had received permission to build a <a href="/wiki/Factory_(trading_post)" title="Factory (trading post)">factory</a> at Chinacota, where a Chinese storehouse first stood eighty years before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finl92-226_320-0"><a href="#cite_note-finl92-226-320">[295]</a></sup> In the late 16th century, <a href="/wiki/Juan_Gonz%C3%A1lez_de_Mendoza" title="Juan Gonz&#225;lez de Mendoza">Juan Gonz&#225;lez de Mendoza</a> wrote that "it is plainly seene, that [the Chinese] did come with shipping into the Indies, having conquered al that is from China, unto the farthest part thereof. . . . So that at this day there is great memory of them . . . in the kingdom of Calicut, where be so many trees and fruits . . . were brought thither by the Chinos when that they were lords and governours of that countrie."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-321"><a href="#cite_note-321">[296]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In November 1997 during a <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a> speech, President <a href="/wiki/Jiang_Zemin" title="Jiang Zemin">Jiang Zemin</a> praised Admiral Zheng He for spreading Chinese culture abroad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lee10-104_322-0"><a href="#cite_note-lee10-104-322">[297]</a></sup> Many present-day Chinese people perceive these historical events, namely the voyages, were conducted in accordance with Confucian ideals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lee10-104_322-1"><a href="#cite_note-lee10-104-322">[297]</a></sup> Since 2005, commemorating the Ming treasure voyages, China has annually celebrated its <a href="/wiki/China_National_Maritime_Day" title="China National Maritime Day">National Maritime Day</a> on 11 July.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-do12-54_323-0"><a href="#cite_note-do12-54-323">[298]</a></sup> That year also marked the 600th anniversary of Zheng He\'s maiden voyage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-do12-54_323-1"><a href="#cite_note-do12-54-323">[298]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-324"><a href="#cite_note-324">[299]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although the present-day popular narrative may emphasize the peaceful nature of the voyages, especially in terms of the absence of territorial conquest and subjugation, it overlooks the heavy <a href="/wiki/Militarization" title="Militarization">militarization</a> of the Ming treasure fleet to exercise <a href="/wiki/Power_projection" title="Power projection">power projection</a> and thereby promote its interests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-325"><a href="#cite_note-325">[300]</a></sup> In present-day Chinese political discourse, with rising developments in China\'s maritime capabilities and ambitions, the Ming treasure voyages is evoked to underscore a <a href="/wiki/China%27s_peaceful_rise" title="China\'s peaceful rise">peaceful emergence of modern China</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-326"><a href="#cite_note-326">[301]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-no17-221-3_327-0"><a href="#cite_note-no17-221-3-327">[302]</a></sup> It\'s suggested that, by drawing parallels between contemporary China and the historical narrative as provided by these voyages, this political process has several functions for the Chinese nation: it stimulates national pride and shapes national identity, it reaffirms a maritime identity, it legitimizes the development of maritime power, it provides an image of a harmonious and peaceful development, it highlights interconnectedness with the broader world, and it provides contrast to the violent nature of western colonialism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-no17-221-3_327-1"><a href="#cite_note-no17-221-3-327">[302]</a></sup> As such, the Ming treasure voyages play an important narrative role in China\'s desire to change its strategic paradigm to that of a maritime power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-no17-221-3_327-2"><a href="#cite_note-no17-221-3-327">[302]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ming_treasure_voyages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-40"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-40">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In the <i>Taizong Shilu</i>, the imperial order is dated to 17 October 1408 (Dreyer 2007, 62; Duyvendak 1939, 361). In the <i>Mingshi</i>, this date is 7 October 1408 (Duyvendak 1939, 361). However, the imperial order was stated as 1407 in Zheng He\'s inscriptions and Ma Huan\'s book (Dreyer 2007, 62). After correction of the year in the former two works, the order date would be 23 October 1407 derived from the <i>Taizong Shilu</i> (Dreyer 2007, 62; Duyvendak 1939, 364) or 13 October 1407 derived from the <i>Mingshi</i> (Duyvendak 1939, 364).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-47"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-47">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Giving a different account, Chan (1998, 271&#8211;272) states that, during the second voyage between 1408 and 1409, the King of West Java killed 170 members of Zheng He\'s personnel who had come ashore on his rival\'s territory at East Java, so Zheng He was forced to intervene in a military capacity.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-zhangchi-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-zhangchi_50-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-zhangchi_50-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">A <i>zhang</i> was ten <i>chi</i> and a <i>chi</i> was 10.5&#8211;12 inches (Dreyer 2007, 65).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-61"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-61">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Dreyer (2007, 66 &amp; 72&#8211;73) thinks it happened during the outward journey in 1410, but notes that most authorities think it happened during the homeward journey in 1411. Dreyer (2007, 72&#8211;73) also notes that Chinese sources make no mention when the confrontation exactly happened during the course of the third voyage.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-83"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-83">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A total of 19 countries was listed, but Lambri was listed twice, namely as <i>Nanwuli</i> and <i>Nanpoli</i> (Dreyer 2007, 82&#8211;83; Mills 1970, 13). The 18 countries were Champa, Pahang, Java, Palembang, Malacca, Semudera, Lambri, Ceylon, the Maldive Islands, Cochin, Calicut, <i>Shaliwanni</i> (possibly Cannanore), Hormuz, <i>Lasa</i>, Aden, Mogadishu, Brava, and Malindi (Mills 1970, 13).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-101"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-101">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <i>Mingshi</i> states that <i>Ganbali</i> was a little country in the Western Ocean. It has traditionally been identified as Coimbatore, but Cambay in Gujarat or Cape Comorin may also be possible. (Dreyer 2007, 46 &amp; 93&#8211;94)</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-104"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-104">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">On 9 May 1421, lightning struck the emperor\'s new palace in Beijing, causing the Fengtian, Huagai, and Jinshen Halls to be destroyed in a fire (Ray 1987b, 161&#8211;162). For his decision to discontinue the Indian Ocean expeditions, the Yongle Emperor was influenced by views that this disaster was a bad omen and a sign against the voyages (Sen 2016, 612).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-108"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-108">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <i>Taizong Shilu</i> 27 February 1424 entry reports that Zheng He was sent to Palembang. The <i>Xuanzong Shilu</i> 17 September 1425 entry reports that Zhang Funama was sent to Palembang. The later <i>Mingshi</i> compilers seem to have combined these two accounts into one journey. (Dreyer 2007, 96)</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-xiaxiyang-7th-126"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-xiaxiyang-7th_126-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-xiaxiyang-7th_126-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">See Dreyer (2007, 150&#8211;163) and Mills (1970, 14&#8211;18).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-144"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-144">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">P. Pelliot (Cited in Mills 1970, 19) argued that they did not travel with the main fleet to Java. Another authority (Cited in Dreyer 2007, 156&#8211;157) argued for a detachment after Vijaya. Although, Dreyer (2007, 157) argued that there\'s no reason to believe a detachment had happened before Semudera.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-148"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-148">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The following facts attest to this: (1) Ma Huan wrote a very detailed record about Mecca (Dreyer 2007, 158&#8211;159; Mills 1970, 36), (2) the imperial clerk Gu Po wrote in the afterword of the <i>Yingya Shenglan</i> that Ma Huan and Guo Chongli had visited Mecca (Mills 1970, 35&#8211;36 &amp; 41&#8211;42), (3) Ma Huan wrote in his foreword that he spoke of personal observations that were reflected in his book (Mills 1970, 35 &amp; 41), and (4) he desired to go there as he was a Muslim himself (Mills 1970, 36).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-153"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-153">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This is the waters around Poulo Condore and the Con Son Islands (Dreyer 2007, 160; Mills 2007, 17).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-154"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-154">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In the <i>Xia Xiyang</i>, it is recorded: "fifth month, tenth day [28 May 1433]: returning, [the fleet] arrived at the Kunlun Ocean." Dreyer (2007) deems it more likely that the date of 28 May referred to the departure from Malacca. He suggests the possibility that the arrival date at the Kunlun Ocean could have been dropped out in the text, as the word "returning" possibly indicated a departure from a location (similar to the account for Hormuz). He adds that, if the text is accepted as it is, the fleet would have departed from Malacca within a few days and would have traveled at a very slow pace of 16 days along the Champa coast. (Dreyer 2007, 160&#8211;161)</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-156"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-156">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">It recorded Culao Re\'s mountains on 19 June, Nan\'ao Island\'s mountains on 25 June, Dongding Island\'s (Chapel Island) mountains in the evening of 26 June, Qitou Yang (Fodu Channel) on 30 June, <i>Wan Tieh</i> [possibly Damao Island\'s mountains] on 1 July, and the mountains of Daji Island (Gutzlaff Island) and Xiaoji Island (Hen and Chicks) on 6 July (Mills 1970, 17&#8211;18).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-185"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-185">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Major ports in their respective regions included Palembang on the Malaccan Strait, Calicut on the Malabar coast, and Mombasa on the Swahili Coast (see Sen 2016).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-226"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-226">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">There are no exact translations for these military ranks. In this case, the article\'s text follows Mills (1970).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-227"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-227">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">He was probably the principal purser for the fleet (Dreyer 2007, 128).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-228"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-228">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">They were in charge of the reception of foreign envoys to the Chinese capital (Dreyer 2007, 128).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-230"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-230">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">They likely operated heavy (war) equipment (Mills 1970, 32).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-237"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-237">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Dreyer (2007, 123) thinks that the fleet had a total of 255 ships, including the treasure ships, but he also mentions that the figure for 317 ships is credible and the general consensus of most scholars.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-263"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-263">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Circa January and December (Dreyer 2007, 30; Mills 1970, 9)</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-282"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-282">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This date of 15 February 1409 possibly refers to when the trilingual inscription was erected in Galle, indicating that it was put up during the homeward journey of the second voyage (Dreyer 1997, 66). If not, the inscription could have been prepared in China and erected between 1410 when the fleet arrived at Galle to 1411 during the third voyage (Dreyer 2007, 72). Duyvendak (1939, 369) states that the inscription must have been prepared in China on 15 February 1409 and that the inscription must have been erected during the third expedition (1409-1411), as he infers that the 15 February 1409 date is connected to the dates for the conference of honors to two deities, Tianfei (&#22825;&#22915;) on 21 January 1409 and Nanhaishen (&#21335;&#28023;&#31070;) on 15 February 1409.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-287"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-287">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The original work has been lost. Later copies of Ma\'s work have been preserved, but it contains differences due to later editors. These include the <i>Jilu Huibian</i> [&#32000;&#37636;&#24409;&#32232;] version (1617), the <i>Guochao Diangu</i> [&#22283;&#26397;&#20856;&#25925;] version (between 1451 and 1644), the <i>Shengchao Yishi</i> [&#21213;&#26397;&#36986;&#20107;] version (1824), and Zhang Sheng\'s so-called "rifacimento" (1522). (Mills 1970, 37&#8211;40)</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-300"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-300">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Duyvendak (1939, 387) and Mills (1970, 8&#8211;9) made the conclusion that the recorded Palembang journey never happened. However, Dreyer (2007, 96) states that it cannot be proven whether it did or did not happen.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-304"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-304">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Zhang Sheng completely rewrote the <i>Yingya Shenglan</i> into a literary style of composition, while Ma Huan had originally written it in a colloquial style (Mills 1970, 38).</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span (1998)</a>, 233.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-drey7-52-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-drey7-52_25-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-drey7-52_25-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-drey7-52_25-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-drey7-52_25-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-drey7-52_25-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-drey7-52_25-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-drey7-52_25-6"><sup><i><b>g</b></i></sup></a></span>
